And the first sitting, and the two tashahhuds. It was mentioned in al-Dhakhira that the first sitting is sunnah and the second is wajib. And in al-Hidayah: the recitation of tashahhud in the first sitting is sunnah, and in the second it is wajib, but the author (al-Marghinani) did not adopt this. [Footnote 1: His saying: 'the first' means the first in order, not the preceding individual, for the first and second sittings may both be wajib as in the case of the latecomer who has missed three rak'ahs in a four-rak'ah prayer, where he performs three sittings: the first and second are wajib and the last is fard.] The evidence for the obligation of the first sitting and that it is not fard is what Abu Dawud and others narrated: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) once omitted the first sitting forgetfully and performed sajda al-sahw. Had it been fard, it would not have been compensated by that, rather his prayer would be invalid. His saying: 'and the two tashahhuds' i.e. reciting 'al-Tahiyyat lillah...' in the sitting. His saying: 'sunnah' — this is the statement of al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi; obligation is the correct view as in al-Zahiriyya and Manh al-Ghaffar. And in al-Badai' it is said: Most of our scholars have called it sunnah, either because its obligation is known from the sunnah in the sense of act, or because the emphasized sunnah mentioned is in the meaning of wajib. His saying: 'and in al-Hidayah' — it is better to say: it is understood from al-Hidayah, for what he mentioned is understood from his words in the chapter on description of prayer, when enumerating the obligatory acts: like reciting al-Fatiha, adding a sura, maintaining order in repeated actions, the first sitting, and reciting tashahhud in the last... etc. His restriction by saying 'in the last' indicates that reciting tashahhud in the first is not obligatory, based on the principle that restricting a ruling to a condition negates the ruling from others in the expressions of the jurists. But he did not explicitly state anywhere in al-Hidayah that reciting tashahhud in the first is sunnah; rather he explicitly stated its obligation in the chapter on sajda al-sahw of al-Hidayah. Because his saying (Ibn Mas'ud): 'Say: al-Tahiyyat lillah' does not require a distinction in reciting tashahhud in the first and second, rather it requires obligation in both. And since the recitation in the first sitting is wajib, the first sitting is also wajib, not sunnah. His saying: 'because his saying...' this hadith is narrated by al-Bukhari in the wording: 'When one of you prays, let him say: al-Tahiyyat lillah wa al-salawat...' and in Ibn Majah's narration: 'When you sit, say: al-Tahiyyat lillah' and in al-Nasa'i and al-Tahawi's narration: 'When you sit after every two rak'ahs, say: al-Tahiyyat lillah' and in a narration by al-Nasa'i: 'The Messenger of Allah said to us: Say in every sitting...' Hence it is clear that there is no need to deduce from the absolute command and say that it is absolute obligating in both sittings without distinction, as the commentator did, for many narrations explicitly mention both sittings. His saying: 'obligates' — objection is raised against obligation: the command is for fard, so why did they not say it is fard? The answer: because the hadith of tashahhud is from ahad hadith, so it does not establish fardiyya but rather obligation. His saying: 'and since it was' — this is a proof for the obligation of the first sitting: since the recitation of tashahhud in the first is wajib, then the first sitting is also wajib, because whatever is necessary for the completion of an obligatory act is itself obligatory, as established in works of usul.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal status of the first sitting (qa’dah) and the recitation of tashahhud during it in prayer. The author begins by citing from al-Dhakhira that the first sitting is sunnah and the second is wajib, and from al-Hidayah that tashahhud in the first is sunnah and in the second wajib, but notes that the original matn author did not adopt this. The commentator clarifies that 'first' here means the first in order, not the individual before the last, because in the case of a latecomer who has missed three rak'ahs, the first two sittings are wajib and the last is fard. The proof for obligation without being fard is the hadith that the Prophet omitted the first sitting forgetfully and performed sajda al-sahw; had it been fard, the prayer would have been invalid and sajda could not compensate. He then states that calling it sunnah is the view of al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi, but the correct position (sahih) is that it is wajib, as found in al-Zahiriyya and Manh al-Ghaffar. A quote from al-Badai' explains that some scholars called it sunnah either because its obligation is derived from an action of the Prophet (not a text) or because the emphasized sunnah is considered like wajib. The commentator then scrutinizes al-Hidayah: while its author (al-Marghinani) listed the first sitting among wajibat in the chapter on salat, he limited tashahhud recitation to the last sitting, implying it is not wajib in the first. However, the commentator argues that al-Marghinani explicitly made tashahhud wajib in the chapter on sajda al-sahw, contradicting the sunnah claim. Stronger evidence comes from the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud commanding tashahhud without distinguishing between sittings, supported by numerous narrations specifying 'in every sitting.' An objection that an absolute command implies fard is answered by noting that the hadith is ahad, so it establishes obligation (wujub) not fard. Finally, the passage deduces that since reciting tashahhud in the first sitting is wajib, the sitting itself is also wajib, based on the usul principle: whatever is necessary to complete an obligatory act is itself obligatory. Thus the relied-upon Hanafi position is that the first sitting and its tashahhud are wajib.

Key Terms
القعدة الأولى (al-qa'dah al-ula): The first sitting in the prayer after the second rak'ah (or after two rak'ahs in a four-rak'ah prayer)
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The recitation of 'al-Tahiyyat lillah...' in the sitting
الوجوب (al-wujub): A level of obligation that is less than fard; derived from zanni evidence and requires sajda sahw if omitted forgetfully

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafi scholars say the first sitting (qa'dah) is wajib and not fard?
A) Because the hadith of tashahhud is mutawatir, establishing fard.
B) Because the Prophet omitted it once and performed sajda sahw, proving it is not fard (since fard cannot be compensated).
C) Because the first sitting is not mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because all Hanafi scholars consider it sunnah.

Key Takeaway
The first sitting and its tashahhud are wajib in the Hanafi school, because ahad hadith and the Prophet's sajda sahw prove it is obligatory but less than fard.

Ruling on the Obligation of Tashahhud and the First Sitting
Commentary on the evidence for wajib status of tashahhud and first sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tashahhud and the first sitting, explain why they are wajib not fard by citing the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud and the Hanafi usul principle regarding khabar al-ahad.

Core Idea
Tashahhud and the first sitting are wajib, not fard, because the evidence is a solitary hadith (khabar al-ahad) which establishes wujub, not fard.

In this passage, the commentator explains the evidence that makes reciting tashahhud and sitting after two rak'ats obligatory in the Hanafi school. We will trace the hadith proof, understand why the ruling is wajib rather than fard, and see how the Hanafi response addresses objections from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Tashahhud (recitation) and the first sitting. The Hanafi ruling: both are wajib.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud with the imperative 'qulu'. Note that multiple narrations explicitly mention 'in every sitting'.
The author emphasizes that the command is general to both sittings.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In Hanafi usul, the imperative form (al-amr) indicates obligation (wujub) when the evidence is zanni. Since this hadith is khabar al-ahad (zanni al-thubut), it establishes wujub, not fard.
This is a key distinction: fard requires qat'i evidence (Quran or mutawatir hadith).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i considers the first sitting sunna, not wajib. The Hanafi response relies on the principle that what is indispensable for the wajib is itself wajib.
The objection that the command might imply fard is rebutted by the nature of the evidence.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position is that both are wajib. The mention of the hadith of Dhul-Yadayn (Prophet standing and then prostrating for sahw) shows that missing the first sitting does not invalidate the prayer, confirming it is not fard.
This practical incident demonstrates the imperfect nature of the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن قوله لا بن مسعود : «قُل : التَّحِيَّاتُ الله»، لا يوجب الفرق في قراءة التشهد في الأولى والثانية، بل يوجب الوجوب في كليهما، ولما كانت - أي القرءاة - في القعدة الأولى واجبة، كانت القعدة الأولى واجبة أيضاً لا سنة). 11 ] قوله : لإن قوله ... الخ ؛ هذا الحديث أخرجه البخاري بلفظ : «إذا صلّى أحدكم فليقل: التحبات الله والصلوات.... الخ ) ، وفي رواية ابن ماجة: «إذا جلستم فقولوا : التحيات لله (۳)، وفي رواية النسائي والطحاوي: «إذا قعدتم في كل ركعتين فقولوا : التحيات لله ...الخ)، وفي رواية للنسائي: «قال لنا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قولوا في كل جلسة. الخ). وبهذا يظهر أنه لا حاجة إلى أن يستدل بالأمر المطلق، ويقال : إنه مطلق يوجب الرجوب في كلتا الجلستين من غير فرق، كما فعله الشارح ، فإن كثيراً من الروايات بذكر الجلستين صراحة.
[٢] قوله : يوجب ؛ الوجوب يرد عليه أنّ الأمر للافتراض، فلم لم يقولوا بافتراضه ويجاب عنه : بأن خبر التشهد من أخبار الآحاد ، فلا تثبت الفرضية بل الوجوب. (۳) قوله : ولما كانت ؛ استدلال على وجوب القعدة الأولى، بأنه لما كانت قراءة التشهد في الأولى واجبة، يلزم منه وجوب القعدة الأولى أيضاً ؛ لأنّ ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به يكون واجباً ، كما ثبت في كتب الأصول.
فإن قلت : فيلزم أن تكون القعدة الأخيرة أيضاً واجبة ؛ لوجوب قراءة التشهد قلت: كلا ؛ فإن ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به يجب أن لا يكون أدنى من الواجب، بأن يكون سنة أو مستحباً، ولا يلزم أن يكون مثله من كل وجه، فلو ثبتت فرضيته بدليل لم يقدح في المقصود.
فإن قلت : فلتكن القعدة الأولى أيضاً فرضاً.
قلت: لولا ما أخرجه أصحاب السنن وغيرهم أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قام من ثنتين ، ولم يجلس ، وسجد للسهو لقلنا بافتراضه، وبهذا يثبت عدم افتراض التشهد أيضاً.
Because his statement, attributed to Ibn Mas'ud: 'Say: al-tahiyyat lillah', does not necessitate a difference in reciting tashahhud in the first and second [sittings], but rather necessitates obligation in both. And since the recitation in the first sitting is obligatory, the first sitting is also obligatory, not sunna. ... [2] His statement: 'obligates'—the obligation is objected to because the command is for fard, so why did they not say it is fard? The answer: because the hadith of tashahhud is from khabar al-ahad, so fard is not established, only obligation. ... Then if you say: it would follow that the last sitting is also obligatory due to the obligation of reciting tashahhud. I say: no, because 'what is indispensable for the obligatory need not be at the same level as the obligatory; it could be sunna or desirable, and it is not necessary that it be equivalent in every respect.' If its fard was established by evidence, it would not harm the purpose. If you say: then let the first sitting be fard. I say: Were it not for what the authors of the Sunan and others narrated that the Prophet stood from two [rak'ahs] and did not sit, and performed prostration of forgetfulness, we would say it is fard. And by this, it is established that tashahhud is not fard.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of tashahhud and the first sitting in prayer. The author explains that the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud commands recitation of tashahhud in general, without distinguishing between the first and second sittings, thus it is obligatory in both. Since tashahhud is wajib in the first sitting, the sitting itself becomes wajib by the principle that 'what is indispensable for the wajib is itself wajib'. However, the author addresses the objection that the command in the hadith could imply fard (farḍiyya). The response is that the hadith is a khabar al-āḥād (a solitary narration), which, in Hanafi uṣūl, establishes wujūb but not farḍiyya, which requires qaṭʿī evidence. The passage then refutes the argument that the last sitting would also be wajib (it is fard, not just wajib), explaining that the indispensability principle does not require the same level of obligation. Finally, the mention of the hadith of Dhul-Yadayn (the Prophet standing from two rak'ahs without sitting and then prostrating for sahw) proves that the first sitting is not fard, otherwise the Prophet would have repeated the prayer rather than performing sahw.

Key Terms
التشهد (al-tashahhud): The recitation of 'al-tahiyyat lillah...' in the sitting of prayer.
القعدة الأولى (al-qa'dah al-ula): The first sitting after two rak'ahs in a prayer of three or more rak'ahs.
خبر الآحاد (khabar al-ahad): A solitary hadith, not reaching the level of tawatur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is reciting tashahhud considered wajib (obligatory) but not fard (compulsory) in the Hanafi school?
A) Because the Prophet never recited it in the first sitting, so it cannot be fard.
B) Because the hadith commanding it is a khabar al-ahad (solitary narration), which establishes wujub but not fard.
C) Because the first sitting is only recommended, so tashahhud cannot be obligatory.
D) Because the Quran does not mention tashahhud, so it is only a sunna.

Key Takeaway
Tashahhud and the first sitting are considered wajib in the Hanafi school due to evidence from khabar al-ahad, distinguishing between fard (invalidates) and wajib (requires compensation).

Ruling of the Salam in Prayer: Wajib or Fard?
Hanafi ruling on salam as wajib, with proof and Shafi'i opposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the salam in prayer, explain the Hanafi proof from the hadith of the sitting imam, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The salam is wajib, not fard—its omission does not invalidate the prayer.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling that the salam (salutation) in prayer is wajib, not fard. This distinction has practical consequences: if one forgets the salam, the prayer remains valid and requires sajdah al-sahw, unlike in the Shafi'i view where the prayer would be invalid. We will analyse the key proofs and the scholarly dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What exactly does the Hanafi school hold regarding the salam? Identify the hukm level (fard/wajib/sunnah), the minimal wording, and the status of turning the face and the second salam.
The Hanafi position is that salam is wajib, the minimal wording is 'al-salam', turning the face is sunnah, and the second salam is also wajib.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school objects and on what grounds? State the Shafi'i view that salam is fard (rukn) and their primary evidence.
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that salam is fard, citing the hadith: 'The key to prayer is purification, its tahrim is the takbir, and its tahlil is the salam.'
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is their dalil? Examine the Shafi'i hadith and understand how they derive that salam is a rukn.
The word 'tahlil' suggests that salam is the integral that ends the prayer, similar to takbir which is a rukn.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut this? Look at the hadith of the sitting imam and the Hanafi interpretation of 'tahlil'.
The Hanafis argue that 'tahlil' means what makes something permissible; it can be a condition, not a pillar. The sitting imam hadith shows that exiting can occur by intention alone, proving salam is not a rukn.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in rulings? Specifically, how does each school rule on forgetting the salam?
Hanafi: prayer is valid, sajdah al-sahw required. Shafi'i: prayer is invalid, must be repeated.

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the ruling of the salam in prayer. The Hanafi position, as stated by the commentator, is that the salam is wajib (obligatory), not fard, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i who considers it fard. The author first presents the Hanafi ruling: the wajib part is the word 'al-salam' itself, while turning the face and the second salam are sunnah. Then he addresses two hypothetical objections. The first objection: if the tashahhud is wajib, then the last sitting would also have to be wajib because it is a precondition for the tashahhud. The response: the sitting is a means to fulfill the wajib, and it can be of a lower category (sunnah or mustahab); it does not have to be equal in obligation. The second objection: why isn't the first sitting also fard? The response: the Prophet ﷺ omitted the first sitting once and performed sajdah al-sahw, which proves it is not fard. The commentator then explains that the minimal salam is wajib, and adding 'alaykum wa rahmatullah' is sunnah. If an imam says only 'al-salam' and a follower says takbir assuming he said the full salam, he does not enter the prayer because he did not hear the full salam. On page 73, the text continues with a hadith used by Shafi'i to claim the salam is fard: 'The key to prayer is purification, its tahrim is the takbir, and its tahlil is the salam.' The Hanafi response: this hadith proves wujub, not fard, because the word 'tahlil' indicates it is what makes the prayer permissible to exit, and it is not necessarily fard. Another hadith is cited: 'When the imam sits for prayer and then has a hadath before speaking, his prayer is complete...' This shows that the completion of prayer occurs by the mere intention to exit (the sitting), not by the salam itself. Therefore, if salam were fard, the prayer would not be complete without it. So the Hanafi position remains that salam is wajib, not fard. The authority weight is from the Zahir al-Riwayah positions, as the text references major Hanafi works like al-Burhan and al-Tajnis wa al-Mazid.

Key Terms
السلام (al-salām): The salutation of the salam in prayer, which is the final obligatory act for exiting prayer. The minimal wajib wording is just 'al-salam'.
فرض (fard): In this context, fard refers to a pillar (rukn) of prayer whose omission invalidates the prayer completely.

Ruling of Qunut in Witr Prayer
Ruling on qunut in witr and its obligatory element.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qunut in witr, identify the wajib element as any supplication, and distinguish between wajib and non-wajib actions.

Core Idea
Qunut in witr: any supplication suffices; raising hands is not wajib.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the Hanafi position on qunut in witr prayer. While many Muslims associate qunut with a specific du'a, the Hanafi school holds that the wajib component is simply making any supplication in the last rak'ah. The passage also addresses details like raising hands and takbir, which are not obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Define qunut linguistically and technically
Identify that qunut in this context means absolute supplication, not a specific wording.
The text explicitly states: 'القنوت لغة مطلق الدعاء'.
2
Determine the wajib element
Recognize that the wajib act is reading any supplication in the last rak'ah of witr, not a specific du'a.
See 'فإن الواجب هو قراءة مطلق الدعاء'.
3
Identify what is not wajib
Note that raising hands and saying takbir at the start of qunut are not wajib, as indicated by the phrase 'ليس بواجب'.
The commentator affirms this as 'الصحيح'.
4
Understand the authority weight
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and further verified by later authorities like the author of al-Bahr.
Cross-madhhab: other schools consider it sunnah.

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling of qunut in witr prayer. The commentator explains that 'qunut' linguistically means any supplication (du'a), and what is wajib in witr is to make any du'a, not a specific prescribed wording. Most Hanafis recite the well-known du'a 'Allahumma inna nasta'inuka...', but this is not a required text. The key point: the wajib act is to recite any supplication in the last rak'ah of witr. The commentator also clarifies that raising the hands during qunut and saying takbir at its beginning are not wajib; they are merely sunnah or mustahab. The authority weight for the ruling that qunut is wajib comes from the Hanafi school's reliance on hadith and the practice of the Companions. The text cites Ghunyat al-Mustamli and al-Bahr as sources.

Key Terms
قنوت (qunūt): Linguistically means absolute supplication; in fiqh, it refers to the supplication recited in the last rak'ah of witr prayer.
الوتر (al-witr): The odd-numbered prayer performed after the night prayer, consisting of an odd number of rak'ahs (usually 3).

The Six Extra Takbirs in Eid Prayer: Ruling and Compensation
Hanafi ruling on the six extra takbirs as wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the six extra takbirs in Eid prayer and identify the consequence of omitting any one of them.

Core Idea
Each of the six extra takbirs in Eid prayer is wajib; omitting any one requires sajdah al-sahw.

In the Eid prayer, the imam and congregation recite additional takbirs beyond the normal transitional takbirs. The Hanafi school rules that each of these six extra takbirs is individually obligatory (wajib). This means if you forget one, you must perform the prostration of forgetfulness. Let's understand the ruling and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the extra takbirs in the Eid prayer. According to the Hanafi school, each of these six extra takbirs is wajib (obligatory).
This ruling applies to both Eid al-Fitr and Eid al-Adha.
2
Count the number of extra takbirs
There are six extra takbirs: three in the first rak'ah after the opening takbir, and three in the second rak'ah after the takbir for standing up.
These are in addition to the regular takbirs of the prayer.
3
State the consequence of omission
If any one of these wajib takbirs is omitted, the prayer remains valid but sajdah al-sahw (prostration of forgetfulness) becomes obligatory to compensate for the omission.
If omitted intentionally, the person is sinful but the prayer is still valid after performing sajdah al-sahw.

According to the Hanafi school, each of these six takbirs is wajib (obligatory). If a person omits any one of them intentionally or forgetfully, sajdah al-sahw is required to compensate. The text cites the authority of al-Qunyah, al-Nihayah, and al-Kifayah. The incomplete phrase at the end likely continues with explanation, but the essential ruling is clear.

Key Terms
تكبيرات العيدين (takbīrāt al-ʿīdayn): The six additional takbirs (saying 'Allahu akbar') in the Eid prayer, beyond the regular takbirs of the prayer.
سجد السهو (sajdat al-sahw): Prostration of forgetfulness performed to compensate for an omission or addition in prayer.

Proof that the Final Salām is Wajib, not Fard
Hanafi proof that closing salām is wajib using a hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers the final salām wajib and not fard, and evaluate the dalil from the hadith.

Core Idea
The closing salām is wajib, not fard, because the prayer can be complete without it.

In this lesson, we examine a key proof that distinguishes the Hanafi position on the final salām from that of the Shafi'i school. The commentator uses a hadith to argue that salām cannot be a fard (pillar) because the prayer is considered complete even if the imam exits the prayer state without uttering it. Understanding this proof requires careful attention to the Hanafi usul distinction between fard and wajib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act is the closing salām (taslim). According to the Hanafi school, its hukm is wajib, not fard.
The matn originally said the takbir is fard; the commentator discusses the salām.
2
Locate the dalil
The Hanafi proof is the hadith: 'When the imam sits for prayer and then has an event before speaking, his prayer is complete, and those behind him have completed their prayer.' This is recorded by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Tahawi, and others.
This is an ahad hadith, which in Hanafi usul can establish wajib but not fard.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'فقد تمت صلاته' (his prayer is complete) indicates that the prayer is valid and finished without the salām. If salām were a fard (pillar), the prayer would be invalid without it. Therefore, salām cannot be fard.
The argument relies on the absence of a condition that would invalidate the prayer.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that salām is fard, based on the hadith: 'The key to prayer is purification, its opening (tahrim) is the takbir, and its closing (tahlil) is the taslim.' They interpret 'tahlil' as a pillar.
This shows how different usul principles yield different rulings.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi response: The hadith about the imam sitting proves that the exit from prayer is by one's own action (sitting with intention), not by the utterance of salām. Thus salām is only wajib, established by a hadith ahad, while fard requires definitive evidence.
The mufta bihi position is that salām is wajib; this is the relied-upon view in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّه فرض عنده
[١] قوله : فرض عنده ; لحديث: مفتاح الصلاة الطهور، وتحريمها التكبير، وتحليلها التسليم ، ونحن نثبت الوجوب به، ونستدل بحديث : «إذا قعد الإمام للصلاة فأحدث قبل أن يتكلم فقد تمت صلاته، ومن كان خلفه ممن أتم الصلاة ) ، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي والطحاوي وغيرهم، فإنّه يدلّ على أن الفرض هو الخروج بصنعه، ولو كان السلام فرضاً لما حكم بتمامية الصلاة بدونه.
Then it is fard according to him. [1] His statement: 'fard according to him' due to the hadith: 'The key to prayer is purification, its opening (tahrim) is the takbir, and its closing (tahlil) is the taslim.' We affirm obligation (wujub) by it, and we argue with the hadith: 'When the imam sits for prayer and then has an event (hadath) before speaking, his prayer is complete, and those behind him have completed their prayer.' Recorded by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Tahawi, and others. It indicates that the obligatory (fard) is exiting by one's own action, and if the salām were fard, it would not have judged the prayer complete without it.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi proof establishing that the final salām is not fard (pillar) but wajib (necessary). The commentator explains the matn's claim that the takbir is fard, and then uses a hadith to argue that the salām cannot be fard. The hadith states that if an imam sits for the final tashahhud and then has an event that breaks wudu before he speaks the salām, his prayer is still considered complete. This proves that leaving the prayer state (tahlil) is achieved by his own action of sitting and intending to exit, not by the utterance of salām. The Hanafi school therefore holds that salām is wajib, not fard. The proof is based on a hadith adduced by the hanafiyyah. The passage is a typical example of Hanafi istidlal using a hadith to differentiate between fard (established by definitive evidence) and wajib (established by probable evidence).

Key Terms
فرض (fard): In this context, fard means a pillar (rukn) that invalidates the prayer if omitted.
السلام (al-salam): The closing salutation of the prayer, saying 'السلام عليكم ورحمة الله'.
وجوب (wujub): Obligation, but at a level lower than fard; established by probable evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

The Wajib of Qunut in Witr: Definition and Scope
Definition and ruling of qunut in witr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qunut in witr and distinguish between the wajib element (absolute supplication) and non-wajib elements (specific formula, raising hands, takbir).

Core Idea
Qunut in witr is wajib with any du'a; a specific formula is not required.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the definition of qunut in the context of witr prayer. Many Hanafis are accustomed to reciting a particular supplication, but the commentator emphasizes that the wajib is simply any supplication in the last rak'ah. This lesson unpacks that distinction and identifies what is and is not required.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic meaning of qunut
Qunut literally means 'absolute supplication'. The passage explicitly states that this general meaning is intended, not a specific formula.
2
Identify the wajib element
The passage declares that reciting any supplication (mutilaq al-du'a) in the last rak'ah of witr is wajib. This is the minimum obligation.
3
Recognize what is not wajib
The commentator clarifies that raising the hands and saying takbir at the start of qunut are not wajib. The relied-upon view is that they are not obligatory.
4
Note the authorities
The ruling is supported by references to 'Ghunyat al-Mustamli' and verified by the author of 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : وقنوت الوتر ؛ القنوت لغة ; مطلق الدعاء، وهو المراد هاهنا لا خصوص الدعاء الذي تقرأه أكثر الحنفية من : اللهم إنا نستعينك ونستغفرك ... الخ ; فإن الواجب هو قراءة مطلق الدعاء في الركعة الآخرة من الوتر. كذا في «غنية المستملي» وغيره.
وفي الاكتفاء عليه إشارة إلى أنَّ رفع اليدين عند القنوتِ والتكبير عند ابتدائه ليس
بواجب، وهو الصحيح، كما حققه صاحب «البحر» وغيره.
[2] His statement: 'And the qunut of witr'; qunut linguistically means absolute supplication, and that is what is intended here, not specifically the supplication that most Hanafis recite: 'Allahumma inna nasta'inuka...' etc. For the obligatory (wajib) is reciting an absolute supplication in the last rak'ah of witr. Thus in 'Ghunyat al-Mustamli' and others. And in its sufficiency there is an indication that raising the hands during qunut and the takbir at its beginning is not obligatory, and that is the correct view, as verified by the author of 'al-Bahr' and others.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines qunut in the context of witr prayer. The commentator clarifies that qunut linguistically means any supplication (du'a), and it is the absolute supplication that is wajib (obligatory) in the last rak'ah of witr, not the particular formula often recited by Hanafis (e.g., 'Allahumma inna nasta'inuka'). Therefore, one may recite any du'a. The commentator also notes that according to the relied-upon view, raising the hands during qunut and the takbir at its beginning are not obligatory (wajib). This was verified by the author of 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' and others. The passage is a concise ruling on the minimum requirement of qunut.

Key Terms
القنوت (al-qunut): Any supplication recited in the last rak'ah of witr prayer.
الواجب (al-wajib): An obligation that is less than fard; omission requires sajdat al-sahw.

The Six Additional Takbirs of Eid Prayer: Wajib or Sunnah?
Ruling on the six additional takbirs in Eid prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of the six additional takbirs in Eid prayer according to the Hanafi school and explain the istihsan reasoning that makes them wajib.

Core Idea
Each of the six additional takbirs in Eid is wajib; omission requires sajdat al-sahw.

This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āyah addresses the six additional takbirs in the two Eid prayers. The commentator clarifies that each takbir is wajib (obligatory), not sunnah, and that missing any requires prostration of forgetfulness. The text also reveals the underlying juristic reasoning: although qiyas would classify them as sunnah because they are utterances, istihsan (juristic preference) elevates them to wajib due to their integral connection to the whole prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Determine what is being ruled upon: the six additional takbirs in Eid prayer (three in each rak'ah).
These are separate from the opening takbir and the takbir for bowing.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Each of these takbirs is wajib. This is the position of the Hanafi school, as recorded in authoritative texts like al-Qunyah, al-Nihayah, and al-Kifayah.
3
Identify the consequence of omission
If any one of these takbirs is omitted (even forgetfully), sajdat al-sahw becomes obligatory to compensate for the deficiency.
This is because omission of a wajib element requires prostration of forgetfulness.
4
Compare with the qiyas position
According to qiyas (analogy), these takbirs would be sunnah because they are utterances (aqwal), and the general principle is that utterances in prayer are sunnah (not wajib).
This is the hypothetical default ruling if qiyas were applied without istihsan.
5
Understand the istihsan reasoning
Istihsan overrides qiyas because these takbirs are attached to the entire prayer. Their omission creates a deficiency in the completeness of the prayer, so they are elevated to wajib.
The phrase 'they are attached to the entire prayer' (tudāfu ilā jamī‘ al-salāh) is the key justification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] قوله : وتكبيرات العيدين ; أي الستَّةُ ،الزوائد، كلّ منها واجب، فلو ترك واحداً منها وجب سجود السهو ذكره في «القُنية»، وذكر في «النهاية» و«الكفاية»: إنّ القياس أن يكون قنوت الوتر وتكبير العيد سنة ; لأنَّ الأصل في الأقوال السنية، وجه الاستحسان : أنها تضاف إلى جميع الصلاة ، يقال : قنوت الوتر، وتكبيرات العيدين، فبتركه يتمكن النقصان في تمام الصلاة.
[3] His statement: 'And the takbirs of the two Eids'; meaning the six additional takbirs, each of them is wajib. If one omits any of them, sajdat al-sahw becomes obligatory. Mentioned in 'al-Qunyah', and mentioned in 'al-Nihayah' and 'al-Kifayah': The analogy (qiyas) would be that the qunut of witr and the takbir of Eid are sunnah, because the principle is that words (aqwal) are sunnah. But the reason for istihsan is that they are attached to the entire prayer; one says 'qunut of witr' and 'takbirs of the two Eids', so by leaving them a deficiency occurs in the completeness of the prayer.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on the additional takbirs in Eid prayer. The commentator explains that the six extra takbirs (three in the first rak'ah after the opening takbir and three in the second after the takbir for standing) are each wajib. If any is missed, sajdat al-sahw is required. The commentator also notes that according to qiyas (analogy), these takbirs would be sunnah because they are utterances (aqwal) and the general principle is that utterances in prayer are sunnah. However, istihsan (juristic preference) overrides the qiyas because these takbirs are attached to the whole prayer and their omission leaves a deficiency. This is why they are considered wajib. The sources cited are 'al-Qunyah', 'al-Nihayah', and 'al-Kifayah'.

Key Terms
تكبيرات العيدين (takbirat al-'idayn): The six additional takbirs recited in the two Eid prayers (three in the first rak'ah, three in the second).
سجود السهو (sajdat al-sahw): Prostration of forgetfulness required when an obligatory (wajib) element is omitted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Six; each is wajib

D) Three; wajib

Key Takeaway
The six additional takbirs in Eid prayer are each wajib; omission necessitates sajdat al-sahw.

Recitation in First Two Rak'ahs and the Obligatory Nature of Tranquility
Ruling on recitation and tranquility with proof and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recitation in the first two rak'ahs and explain why tranquility (itminan) is wajib, not fard, using the hadith 'Pray, for you have not prayed' and the Quranic command.

Core Idea
Recitation is wajib in the first two rak'ahs; tranquility is wajib, not fard, based on an ahad hadith.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies two key aspects of prayer: the obligation of recitation in the first two rak'ahs of fard prayers, and the legal nature of tranquility (itminan) in ruku and sujud. The commentator explains that missing recitation in the first two requires sajdat al-sahw, and that tranquility is wajib because it is established by a single-narrator hadith while the Quranic command for ruku and sujud is absolute. The passage also resolves the apparent disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa, affirming that the Hanafi position is consistent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the passage
Read the passage and note that it first discusses the obligation of recitation in specific rak'ahs, then the obligation of tranquility and its proof.
The passage is structured in numbered sections: [11], [2], [3].
2
For recitation: Determine when it is wajib and when sajdat al-sahw is required
In fard prayers of 3 or 4 rak'ahs, recitation is wajib in the first two; in 2-rak'ah fard, in both; in nafl and witr, in all rak'ahs. Leaving recitation in the first two and reciting in the last two obligates sajdat al-sahw.
This is a detailed application of the general rule.
3
For tranquility: Locate the hadith proof and analyze its use
The hadith 'Pray, for you have not prayed' is cited. Recognize that it is a khabar al-wahid.
Hanafi usul holds that khabar al-wahid establishes wujub, not fard.
4
Understand the Hanafi usul principle: ahad hadith establishes wujub
The Quranic command for ruku and sujud is absolute (mutlaq), so minimal movement fulfills the fard. The added tranquility is wajib via the ahad hadith.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the difference between fard and wajib.
5
Contrast the Shafi'i position and Abu Yusuf's terminology
Al-Shafi'i considers itminan as a rukn (fard) whose omission invalidates prayer. Abu Yusuf calls it 'fard' but means 'practical fard' (wajib), so no real difference with Abu Hanifa.
The thamarah al-khilaf is that omitting itminan does not invalidate the prayer in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١١ ] قوله : وتعيين الأوليين ; أي من الفرائض الرباعي والثلاثي، وأما الفرضُ الثنائي فالقراءة فرض في ركعتيه، وكذا في جميع ركعات النفل، وركعات الوتر، فلو ترك القراءة في الأوليين وقرأ في الأُخْرَيَّين وجب سجود السهو، وسيجيء تفصيله في فصل القراءة» إن شاء الله تعالى.
[٢]قوله : فإنه فرض عندهما ; لما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لمن صلى بغير تعديل الأركان : «صل فإنك لم تصل» (۳) ، أخرجه البخاري والترمذي والنسائي وغيرهم. ونحن نستدل به على إثباتِ الوجوب بناءً على أن الأمر الوارد بالركوع والسجود في القرآن مطلق، فيكون أدناه فرضاً، وما ثبت بخبر الواحد يكون واجباً، وفي المقام أبحاث بسطناها في «السعاية»(۳).
ثم فرضيّة الاعتدال . عند الشافعي بوصف الركنية، حتى لو تركه بطلت صلاته عنده ، وأما عند أبي يوسف له فالمراد بالفرض الفرضُ العملي، وهو الواجب بعينه، فلا خلاف بينه وبين شيخه في الحقيقة. كذا حققه ابن الهمام في «فتح القدير )
(٤) وابن نجيم في «البحر». 
[٣] قوله : وقُدر ; بصيغة المجهول، من التقدير ; أي قدر الاطمئنان الواجب بمقدار تسبيحة واحدة من تسبيحات الركوع والسجود ، وما زاد عليه مستحب.
[11] His statement: 'And the designation of the first two'; i.e., from the four-rak'ah and three-rak'ah fard prayers. As for the two-rak'ah fard, recitation is fard in both of its rak'ahs. Similarly in all rak'ahs of nafl and witr. If one leaves recitation in the first two and recites in the last two, sajdat al-sahw becomes obligatory. Its detail will come in the chapter on recitation, God willing.
[2] His statement: 'For it is fard according to them'; because it is narrated that the Prophet ﷺ said to the one who prayed without tranquility (ta'dil al-arkan): 'Pray, for you have not prayed.' Recorded by al-Bukhari, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, and others. We argue with it to establish obligation (wujub) based on the fact that the command for ruku and sujud in the Quran is absolute (mutlaq), so its minimal level is fard, and what is established by a khabar al-wahid is wajib. There are discussions which we have expanded in 'al-Si'ayah'. Then, the fardiyyah of moderation (i'tidal) according to al-Shafi'i is as a pillar (rukn), so if one omits it, his prayer is invalid. As for Abu Yusuf, what is meant by fard is 'practical fard' (al-fard al-'amali), which is exactly the same as wajib. So there is no real difference between him and his teacher. This is verified by Ibn al-Humam in 'Fath al-Qadir' and Ibn Nujaym in 'al-Bahr'. 
[3] His statement: 'And it was measured'; in passive form, from taqdir; meaning the obligatory tranquility was measured by the amount of one tasbih from the tasbihs of ruku and sujud, and what exceeds that is mustahabb.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage covers two rulings: (1) the obligation of recitation in the first two rak'ahs of fard prayers (of 3 or 4 rak'ahs) and (2) the obligation of tranquility (itminan) in ruku and sujud. For recitation, the commentator states that in 3- or 4-rak'ah fard prayers, recitation is wajib in the first two rak'ahs; in 2-rak'ah fard, in both; in nafl and witr, in all rak'ahs. Missing recitation in the first two requires sajdat al-sahw if recited elsewhere. For tranquility, the commentator explains the proof: the hadith 'Pray, for you have not prayed' is used to show that tranquility is wajib (not fard) because the command for ruku and sujud in the Quran is absolute (not specifying tranquility), so the minimal fulfillment is fard (i.e., any movement), but the extra tranquility is wajib based on the ahad hadith. The commentator also clarifies a dispute: al-Shafi'i considers tranquility (i'tidal) as a rukn (fard) whose omission invalidates prayer, while Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifa that it is wajib (not rukn) and the term 'fard' used by Abu Yusuf is practical fard, essentially synonymous with wajib. Finally, the minimal amount of obligatory tranquility is one tasbih (saying 'subhan Allah' once); more is recommended.

Key Terms
الاطمئنان (al-itminan): Tranquility or stillness in ruku and sujud after assuming the posture.
تعديل الأركان (ta'dil al-arkan): To perform the pillars with proper composure and tranquility.
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A hadith narrated by one or a few chains, not reaching tawatur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Tranquility in ruku and sujud is wajib, not fard, and its minimum measure is one tasbih; omitting recitation in the first two rak'ahs requires sajdat al-sahw.

Recitation in the First Two Rak'ahs of Fard Prayers
Explaining the ruling of recitation in the first two rak'ahs of fard prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on where recitation must occur in fard prayers and identify when sajda al-sahw is required.

Core Idea
In fard prayers, recitation is wajib in the first two rak'ahs; omitting it there requires sajda al-sahw.

This lesson explains a key ruling from the matn: that recitation is specifically assigned to the first two rak'ahs of fard prayers. Understanding this helps ensure correct prayer and proper compensation when mistakes occur.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states 'تعيين الأوليين للقراءة' meaning recitation is determined for the first two rak'ahs. In Hanafi fiqh, this is a wajib for three- and four-cycle fard prayers.
2
Distinguish the prayer types
For two-cycle fard prayers, recitation is fard in both rak'ahs. For nafl and witr, recitation is fard in every rak'ah.
3
Note the consequence of omission
If one leaves recitation in the first two and recites in the last two, sajda al-sahw becomes wajib.
This indicates the level is wajib, not fard, because omission does not invalidate the prayer.
4
Recognize the scope
This rule applies specifically to fard prayers of three or four rak'ahs. For other prayers, the rule differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعيين الأوليين للقراءة، وتعديل الأركان
قوله : وتعيين الأوليين؛ أي من الفرائض الرباعي والثلاثي، وأما الفرضُ الثنائي فالقراءة فرض في ركعتيه، وكذا في جميع ركعات النفل، وركعات الوتر، فلو ترك القراءة في الأوليين وقرأ في الأُخْرَيَّين وجب سجود السهو، وسيجيء تفصيله في فصل القراءة إن شاء الله تعالى.

This is a ruling statement clarifying the matn phrase 'تعيين الأوليين للقراءة'. The full detail is promised later in the chapter on recitation.

The Obligation of Tranquility in Prayer: Wājib or Farḍ?
Proving the obligation of tranquility (itminan) in bowing and prostration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on tranquility (itminan) in rukūʿ and sujūd, cite the evidence, and distinguish the Hanafi position from that of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Tranquility is wājib, not farḍ — a terminological difference that affects validity.

This lesson addresses a classic inter-madhhab dispute over tranquility (itminan) in bowing and prostration. The Hanafi school holds it is wājib, while Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i consider it farḍ. We will dissect the evidence, the reasoning behind each position, and see how later Hanafi masters reconciled the apparent disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute
Locate the act under dispute: tranquility (itminan) in rukūʿ and sujūd. The text states it is farḍ according to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, but the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa) says it is wājib.
The term 'farḍ' in this context is used differently by different scholars.
2
Analyze the Dalīl
Examine the hadith of the man who prayed without tranquility: the Prophet ﷺ said 'صل فإنك لم تصل'. How does this prove obligation? In Hanafi uṣūl, an imperative from a khabar al-wāḥid indicates wujūb, not farḍiyya.
3
Reconcile the Positions
Abu Yusuf's 'farḍ' is interpreted as al-farḍ al-ʿamalī (operational farḍ), which is identical to wājib. Thus, there is no substantive difference between him and his teacher. The real disagreement is with al-Shafi'i, who considers it a rukn.
Ibn al-Humam and Ibn Nujaym confirm this reconciliation.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: if omitted, in the Hanafi school sajda al-sahw is required; in the Shafi'i school the prayer is invalid. This distinction matters for daily practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعديل الأركان خلافاً لأبي يوسف، والشافعي، فإنه فرض عندهما، وهو الاطمئنان في الركوع، وكذا في السجود.
قوله : فإنه فرض عندهما؛ لما روي أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لمن صلى بغير تعديل الأركان: «صل فإنك لم تصل» أخرجه البخاري والترمذي والنسائي وغيرهم. ونحن نستدل به على إثبات الوجوب بناءً على أن الأمر الوارد بالركوع والسجود في القرآن مطلق، فيكون أدناه فرضاً، وما ثبت بخبر الواحد يكون واجباً. ثم فرضية الاعتدال عند الشافعي بوصف الركنية، حتى لو تركه بطلت صلاته عنده، وأما عند أبي يوسف فالمراد بالفرض الفرض العملي، وهو الواجب بعينه، فلا خلاف بينه وبين شيخه في الحقيقة. كذا حققه ابن الهمام في «فتح القدير» وابن نجيم في «البحر».
قوله : وقدر؛ بصيغة المجهول من التقدير؛ أي قدر الاطمئنان الواجب بمقدار تسبيحة واحدة من تسبيحات الركوع والسجود، وما زاد عليه مستحب.
And adjusting the pillars, contrary to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, for it is farḍ according to them, and it is tranquility (itminan) in bowing and likewise in prostration. He said: 'For it is farḍ according to them' due to the report that the Prophet ﷺ said to the one who prayed without adjusting the pillars: 'Pray, for you have not prayed.' It was narrated by al-Bukhari, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, and others. We use it to prove obligation, based on the fact that the command for bowing and prostration in the Quran is absolute, so its minimum is farḍ, and what is established by khabar al-wāḥid is wājib. Then the farḍiyya of iʿtidāl according to al-Shafi'i is by way of rukn, such that if he omits it, his prayer becomes invalid according to him. As for Abu Yusuf, the meaning of farḍ is operational farḍ, which is the same as wājib, so there is no real difference between him and his teacher. Thus verified by Ibn al-Humam in Fatḥ al-Qadīr and Ibn Nujaym in al-Baḥr. He said: 'And it is estimated' – in the passive voice, meaning the required tranquility is estimated at one tasbīḥ from the tasbīḥs of rukūʿ and sujūd, and what is more than that is mustaḥabb.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the obligation of tranquility (itminan) in rukūʿ and sujūd. The matn states it is farḍ according to Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i, but Abu Hanifa holds it is wājib. The commentator supports the Hanafi position by citing the hadith of the man who prayed improperly; the Prophet ﷺ commanded him to repeat, indicating obligation (wujūb), not farḍiyya. The reasoning: the Quranic command for rukūʿ and sujūd is absolute, so the minimum is farḍ, but the hadith of the man is khabar al-wāḥid, which establishes wājib in Hanafi uṣūl. Then Ibn al-Humam and Ibn Nujaym reconcile the positions: Abu Yusuf's use of 'farḍ' is in the sense of operational farḍ (al-farḍ al-ʿamalī), which is identical to wājib in the Hanafi school. Finally, the required tranquility is estimated as the time to say one tasbīḥ (e.g., 'subḥān Allāh'), with extra being mustaḥabb.

Key Terms
الاطمئنان (al-itminān): Tranquility; a brief pause in rukūʿ and sujūd sufficient to say one tasbīḥ
الفرض العملي (al-farḍ al-ʿamalī): A term used by Abu Yusuf meaning an obligatory act that must be performed, identical to wājib in the Hanafi terminological hierarchy

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person forgetfully omits tranquility (itminan) in rukūʿ, what is the ruling?
A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) Sajda al-sahw is wajib.
C) The prayer is valid and no compensation is needed.
D) The prayer must be repeated only if intentional.

Key Takeaway
Tranquility in rukūʿ and sujūd is wājib in the Hanafi school; its omission requires sajda al-sahw but does not invalidate the prayer.

Qunut of Witr and Takbirs of 'Eid as Sunnah
Establishing sunnah status via qiyas and istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school classifies qunut of witr and 'Eid takbirs as sunnah mu'akkadah and apply the method of istihsan to derive this ruling.

Core Idea
Qunut and 'Eid takbirs are sunnah mu'akkadah because they are supererogatory statements attached to the prayer's completeness.

This passage addresses the legal classification of qunut in witr and the extra takbirs in 'Eid prayer. While some might assume they are obligatory, the Hanafi school demonstrates through both qiyas and istihsan that they are sunnah mu'akkadah. Understanding this distinction is crucial for knowing the consequence of omission – the prayer remains valid but incomplete in perfection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and the qiyas ruling
Identify the two acts: qunut in witr and the extra takbirs in 'Eid prayer. According to qiyas, all supererogatory statements in prayer are sunnah by default. This gives a preliminary ruling of sunnah.
The default for extra dhikr in salah is sunnah unless specified otherwise.
2
Apply istihsan to refine the ruling
Istiḥsān (juristic preference) is then applied because these acts are linguistically attached to the entire prayer (e.g., 'qunut of witr'). Omitting them creates a deficiency in the prayer's completeness (kamāl), not in its validity. This reinforces the ruling of sunnah mu'akkadah.
Istiḥsān overrides any possible doubt that omission might make them wājib.
3
Understand the consequence of omission
If a person omits qunut or the 'Eid takbirs, the prayer is still valid (sahīh) and does not require sajda al-sahw. However, leaving them without a valid reason is disliked (makrūh tanzīhan) because it diminishes the perfection of the prayer.
This is the practical application: no need to repeat or prostrate for forgetfulness.
4
Note cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider qunut in witr as sunnah but after ruku', or some earlier views might consider it wājib. The Hanafi position is clear: it is sunnah mu'akkadah based on this istihsan reasoning.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate and the Hanafi tarjih is based on textual and rational preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس أن يكون قنوت الوتر وتكبير العيد سنة؛ لأنَّ الأصل في الأقوال السنية. وجه الاستحسان : أنها تضاف إلى جميع الصلاة، يقال : قنوت الوتر، وتكبيرات العيدين، فبتركه يتمكن النقصان في تمام الصلاة.
Analogical reasoning is that qunut of witr and takbīr of 'Eid are sunnah, because the principle regarding supererogatory statements is sunnah. The basis of istiḥsān: they are attributed to the entire prayer. It is said: 'qunut of witr' and 'takbīrs of the two 'Eids'. So by leaving them, deficiency can occur in the completeness of the prayer.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the proof for why qunut in witr and the takbīrs in 'Eid prayer are sunnah mu'akkadah. The text explains that qiyās (analogy) suggests they should be sunnah because all supererogatory utterances in prayer are sunnah by default. Then istiḥsān (juristic preference) reinforces this: these acts are linked to the entire prayer (e.g., 'qunut of witr' means it is part of witr prayer), so omitting them would create a deficiency in the prayer's completeness. Thus, they are established as sunnah mu'akkadah, not wājib.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method used to depart from qiyas in favor of a stronger evidence or custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling of qunut in witr prayer according to the Hanafi school?
A) Wājib
B) Sunnah mu'akkadah
C) Farḍ
D) Mubāḥ

Key Takeaway
Qunut in witr and 'Eid takbirs are sunnah mu'akkadah; omission does not invalidate the prayer but leaves it incomplete and is disliked.

The Term 'Arkan' in Tranquility Between Prostrations
Clarifying that itminan between bowing and prostration is included in 'adjusting the pillars' by a broad definition of 'arkan'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the technical meaning of 'arkan' (invalidating pillars) and the broader meaning (parts of the prayer) as used in Hanafi texts.

Core Idea
'Pillars' can mean two things: the essential parts that invalidate prayer, or any part of the prayer in a general sense.

This passage addresses a potential confusion. The original source mentioned tranquility in the sitting between prostrations as part of 'adjusting the pillars.' Some objected because the sitting is not a rukn (pillar) by consensus. The commentator clarifies that 'arkan' here means all parts of the prayer, not just the invalidating ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Recognize that the sitting between prostrations is not a technical rukn (its omission does not invalidate). So why is it included in 'adjusting the pillars'?
2
Locate the commentator's answer
The commentator redefines 'arkan' as 'parts of the prayer in an absolute sense.'
This is a broader usage than the technical term.
3
Note the consequence
Tranquility in any part of the prayer is part of adjusting the pillars broadly, even if that part is not a pillar that invalidates.
4
Apply the distinction
In future texts, check whether 'arkan' refers to technical pillars or general components.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وكذا؛ أورد عليه أنّ القومة والجلسة بين السجدتين ليستا من الأركان اتفاقاً، فكيف يدخل الاطمئنان فيهما في تعديل الأركان، وجوابه أنّ المراد بالأركان أجزاء الصلاة مطلقاً لا ما يبطل بتركه الصلاة فقط، فاحفظ هذا، ولا تصغ إلى ما تفوه به الناظرون في هذا المقام على ما أوضحناه في «السعاية».
He said: 'And likewise...' An objection is raised that standing and sitting between the two prostrations are not among the pillars (arkān) by agreement, so how can tranquility in them enter into 'adjusting the pillars'? The answer: what is meant by 'pillars' (arkān) here is the parts of the prayer in an absolute sense, not only those whose omission invalidates the prayer. So memorize this, and do not listen to what observers have uttered in this context, as we have clarified in 'al-Sa'āya'.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This is a critical refinement responding to a potential objection. The matn mentions tranquility in the sitting between prostrations as part of 'adjusting the pillars'. Some object that the sitting (jalsa) between prostrations is not a rukn (pillar) by consensus; its omission does not invalidate the prayer. How then can it be included? The commentator answers that 'pillars' in this context means all parts of the prayer in a general sense, not strictly those whose omission invalidates (i.e., not the technical arkān). This is a clarification of terminology: here 'arkān' is used loosely to mean 'components' of the prayer. The commentator advises to hold this understanding and ignore critics.

Key Terms
الأركان (al-arkān): In this context, the parts of the prayer in an absolute sense, not the technical pillars whose omission invalidates

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The technical pillars that invalidate prayer if omitted.
B) All parts of the prayer in an absolute sense.
C) Only the standing and sitting.
D) The tranquility itself.

Key Takeaway
The term 'arkan' in Hanafi fiqh can be used broadly to refer to any part of the prayer, not only the invalidating pillars.

The Hanafi Distinction Between Farḍ and Wājib
Explaining the Hanafi distinction between farḍ and wājib and the Shafi'i view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between farḍ and wājib, compare it with the Shafi'i view, and identify the practical and creedal implications of the difference.

Core Idea
Farḍ from certain proof; wājib from probable proof; Shafi'is call both farḍ.

This passage clarifies a foundational terminological difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. While both schools recognize that obligations vary in strength of evidence, the Hanafi school has coined distinct terms—farḍ and wājib—to reflect that difference. The passage also shows that the disagreement is largely about names, not about the underlying legal reality.

Guided Path
1
Define farḍ in the Hanafi school
State the Hanafi definition: farḍ is an obligation proven by a qat'ī (definitive) proof. Its denier becomes a disbeliever, and its abandoner deserves punishment.
Qat'ī proof includes the Quran and mutawātir Sunnah.
2
Define wājib in the Hanafi school
State the Hanafi definition: wājib is an obligation proven by a ẓannī (probable) proof, such as a khabar al-wāḥid. Its abandoner deserves punishment, but its denier is not declared a disbeliever.
Ẓannī proof is less certain in transmission or meaning.
3
State the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school does not distinguish between farḍ and wājib; all binding commands are called farḍ regardless of the strength of the proof.
They argue that the term farḍ covers all obligations.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference lies in the ruling on denial: for Hanafis, denying a wājib does not constitute disbelief, while denying a farḍ does. For Shafi'is, denying any obligation is a denial of a farḍ and could be considered disbelief.
In practice, both schools require compensation for omission of any obligation.
5
Analyze the verification (taḥqīq)
The commentator concludes that the dispute is terminological: both schools acknowledge different levels of strength among obligations. The only disagreement is in nomenclature.
This verification resolves the dispute by showing no essential conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وعند الشافعي ... الخ؛ توضيحه : على ما في كتب الأصول أنَّ الفرض عبارة عما ثبت لزومه بدليل قطعي، وحكمه أنه يكفر منكره ويستحق تاركُهُ العقاب، والواجب ما ثبت لزومه بدليل ظنّي كخبر الآحاد ، وحكمه أنه يستحق تاركه العقاب، ولا يكفر جاحده، هذا عندنا. وعند الشافعية لا فرق بين الفرض والواجب، بل كل ما ثبت لزومه يسمونه فرضاً بالدليل الظني كان أو بالقطعي. والتحقيق : أنه لا نزاع بيننا وبينهم في الحقيقة، فإنّ الثابت لزومه بدليل قطعي، والثابت لزومه بدليل ظنّي مختلفان عندهم أيضاً في الأحكام قوة وضعفاً، وإنما ينكرون التسمية بالواجب ويطلقون الفرض على الكل.
He said: 'And according to al-Shafi'i... etc.' Its clarification: According to what is in the books of uṣūl: farḍ is what is proven to be binding by a qaṭʿī (definitive) proof, and its ruling is that its denier becomes a disbeliever and its abandoner deserves punishment. Wājib is what is proven to be binding by a ẓannī (probable) proof, like khabar al-wāḥid, and its ruling is that its abandoner deserves punishment but its denier is not declared a disbeliever. This is our view. According to the Shāfiʿiyya, there is no difference between farḍ and wājib; rather, everything proven to be binding is called farḍ, whether the proof is ẓannī or qaṭʿī. The verification: there is no actual dispute between us and them, because the bindingly proven by qaṭʿī proof and that proven by ẓannī proof are different for them as well in terms of strength and weakness. They only deny the term wājib and apply farḍ to all.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute clarifying the terminology of farḍ and wājib. The Hanafi position defines farḍ as an obligation established by a qat'ī (definitive) proof, such as the Quran or mutawatir Sunnah. Denying farḍ leads to disbelief and abandonment deserves punishment. Wājib is established by a zannī (probable) proof, like ahad hadith; abandonment deserves punishment but denial does not render one a disbeliever. The Shafi'i school does not make this distinction; they call everything farḍ. However, the commentator (following the verification) states that the dispute is only terminological: Shafi'is still differentiate between strong and weak obligations in terms of consequences (e.g., disbelief for denying Quranic commands), but they simply use the word 'farḍ' for both. Thus, the practical differences remain negligible.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-farḍ): An obligation established by a qat'ī (definitive) proof; its denier becomes a disbeliever; its abandoner deserves punishment
الواجب (al-wājib): An obligation established by a ẓannī (probable) proof; its abandoner deserves punishment but its denier is not a disbeliever

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone denies an obligation established by a khabar al-wāḥid, what is the ruling?
A) They become a disbeliever.
B) They are not declared a disbeliever, but are sinful.
C) They are not sinful because it is only sunnah.
D) They become a disbeliever only if they deny it after proof.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between farḍ (qat'ī proof) and wājib (ẓannī proof) is a terminological difference with the Shafi'is, but it has an important creedal implication: denial of wājib does not lead to disbelief.

The Hanafi Classification of Prayer Acts: Fard vs Wajib
Commentary on the Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib and comparison with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fard and wajib according to the Hanafi school, identify the type of evidence for each, and explain the practical consequence of the distinction.

Core Idea
Fard is from qat'i dalil, wajib from zanni; denier of fard is kafir, denier of wajib is not.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah illuminates a key Hanafi usul distinction: the difference between fard and wajib. While both are obligations, their evidential basis differs, leading to different consequences for a denier. The commentator also clarifies the Shafi'i position, showing that the dispute is largely terminological.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi definition of fard
Find where the text defines fard as that which is proven by a definitive (qat'i) proof. Note that its denier becomes a disbeliever.
The text says: 'al-fard ma thabata luzumuhu bi dalil qat'i'.
2
Identify the Hanafi definition of wajib
Find where the text defines wajib as that which is proven by a speculative (zanni) proof. Its denier is punished but not declared a disbeliever.
The text says: 'al-wajib ma thabata luzumuhu bi dalil zanni'.
3
Understand the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'is do not differentiate terminologically; they call both fard. However, the text states that in reality, they acknowledge different strengths.
The commentator says: 'la niza' baynana wa baynahum fi al-haqiqah'.
4
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
The practical difference appears in the ruling on the denier of a wajib: Hanafis do not declare him kafir, while Shafi'is would, but only if the evidence is qat'i according to their own analysis.
Thus the difference is more in terminology than in actual legal effect.

(0)
يُكبر، وصححه في «الهداية ) ، ونسبه في «المبسوط ) إلى عامة مشايخنا، وهو الثابت عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من حديث أبي حميد الساعدي ، أخرجه البخاري وأصحاب السننِ
الأربعة.
Book of Prayer, Description of Prayer. And the audibility and concealment in what is recited aloud and what is recited silently. And other than these are sunnah or mandub. Likewise tranquility between bowing and prostration and between the two prostrations. (And the audibility and concealment in what is recited aloud and what is recited silently. And other than these are sunnah or mandub): i.e., apart from the obligatory acts (fara'id and wajibat), either sunnah or mandub. According to al-Shafi'i, there is no difference between fard and wajib as known in usul al-fiqh; according to him, actions of prayer are either obligatory (fara'id) or sunnan or mustahabbat. [1] His statement: 'Likewise' – objection is raised that standing and sitting between the two prostrations are not pillars (arkan) by agreement, so how does tranquility in them enter into proper performance of pillars? The answer is that by 'pillars' is meant parts of prayer absolutely, not only that whose omission invalidates the prayer. So remember this, and do not pay attention to what the observers said in this context as we clarified in al-Si'ayah (1). (2) His statement: 'i.e., apart from...' – since the apparent wording of the author implies that everything other than the mentioned things is either mandub or sunnah, and it is not so, the commentator intended to correct it with this phrase, meaning that everything apart from the obligatory acts (fara'id and wajibat), whether mentioned before or not, is either emphatic sunnah or recommended acts. [3] His statement: 'According to al-Shafi'i...' – explanation: as in the books of usul, fard is that whose obligation is established by a definitive proof, and its ruling is that its denier becomes a disbeliever and its abandoner deserves punishment; wajib is that whose obligation is established by a speculative proof like khabar al-ahad, and its ruling is that its abandoner deserves punishment but its denier does not become a disbeliever. This is according to us. According to the Shafi'is, there is no difference between fard and wajib; rather, everything whose obligation is established they call fard, whether by speculative or definitive proof. The investigation: there is no real dispute between us and them, for what is established by definitive proof and what is established by speculative proof differ in rulings in terms of strength and weakness according to them as well; they only deny the terminology of wajib and apply fard to everything. (1) Al-Si'ayah (2:144). 76 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah. When he intends to begin, he says the takbir, cutting off [speech], after raising his hands. [Y] (When he intends to begin, he says the takbir, cutting off [speech], after raising his hands.) [1] His statement: 'When he intends to begin' – this is better than the statement of the author of al-Hidayah: 'When he enters into prayer, he says the takbir.' [2] His statement: 'When he intends...' – this applies if he is an imam or alone; if he is a follower, he waits for the imam's takbir, and it is better that he says it after him. If he says it along with the imam's takbir, it is permissible. This is in al-Dhakhirah and al-'Awn. [3] His statement: 'says the takbir' – meaning he says: Allahu Akbar. If he says: Allah, he does not become a prayer-starter in the apparent riwayah, as in al-Dhakhirah. It is recommended that the end of the takbir be with sukun (pause), whether it is the opening takbir or the takbirs of transition; due to the saying of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: 'The takbir is with sukun, the recitation is with sukun, and the call to prayer is with sukun' – narrated by Sa'id ibn Mansur in his Sunan and by al-Tirmidhi. [4] His statement: 'after raising his hands' – this is one of three opinions: that he raises his hands first, then says the takbir. It was corrected in al-Hidayah, and attributed in al-Mabsut to the generality of our scholars, and it is established from the Prophet (peace be upon him) from the hadith of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi, narrated by al-Bukhari and the four Sunan authors.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the description of prayer, specifically addressing two issues: first, the classification of actions in prayer into fard (obligatory by definitive proof), wajib (obligatory by speculative proof), sunnah, and mandub; and second, the ruling and manner of the opening takbir. The commentator clarifies that the Hanafi school distinguishes between fard and wajib, unlike the Shafi'is who call both fard. However, this is largely a terminological difference. Then, the commentary explains that when one intends to begin prayer, he should say 'Allahu Akbar' after raising his hands, cutting off any other speech. For a follower, it is better to say the takbir after the imam. The takbir must include the word 'Akbar'; simply saying 'Allah' is insufficient. It is recommended to pause (sukun) at the end of the takbir. The preferred method of raising hands before the takbir is supported by multiple hadiths, including that of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): An obligation established by definitive (qat'i) evidence; its denier becomes a disbeliever.
الواجب (al-wājib): An obligation established by speculative (zanni) evidence; its abandoner deserves punishment but not disbelief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a person who denies an obligation established by a khabar ahad?
A) He becomes a disbeliever.
B) He is a sinner but not a disbeliever.
C) He is not punished at all.
D) His faith is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib is based on the strength of evidence and affects whether the denier is considered a disbeliever.

The Opening Takbir: Procedure and Rulings
Commentary on the opening takbir and its manner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the opening takbir, describe the recommended method, and explain the follower's timing.

Core Idea
The opening takbir is wajib; the follower should say it after the imam, and raising hands before the takbir is recommended.

This passage details the opening takbir (takbir al-iftitah). It explains that one says 'Allahu Akbar' after raising hands, and the follower should wait until after the imam's takbir. The takbir must include 'Akbar,' and it is recommended to pause at the end. The commentator also cites the hadith of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi for the hand-raising practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wording of the takbir
The text states that the opening takbir is said as 'Allahu Akbar'. Simply saying 'Allah' is insufficient to enter prayer.
The commentator notes that saying only 'Allah' does not start the prayer in the apparent riwayah.
2
Understand the hand-raising issue
There are three opinions on when to raise hands relative to the takbir. The text corrects that hands should be raised first, then the takbir, as supported by the hadith of Abu Humayd.
This is the view of the majority of Hanafi scholars.
3
Learn the follower's timing
If one is a follower, he waits for the imam's takbir, and it is better to say the takbir after the imam. Saying it simultaneously is permissible but less preferred.
The text derives from al-Dhakhirah and al-'Awn.
4
Note the recommended pause
The end of the takbir should be with sukun (a stop), meaning the last letter is not elongated with a vowel. This applies to all takbirs in prayer.
The evidence is the statement of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.
