Timing of Hand-Raising with Takbir in Salah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hand-raising and takbir timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on the timing of hand-raising relative to the opening takbir, identify the evidence for each, and explain why the first opinion is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Raise hands first, then say takbir: the safest and most authentic method according to the majority of Hanafi scholars.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explores a subtle but practically important detail in salah: the timing of hand-raising relative to the opening takbir. While all three methods are valid, the Hanafi school gives preference to raising hands first, based on strong hadith evidence. Understanding this intra-madhhab dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between mufta bihi and other valid views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
List the three sequences for hand-raising and takbir: (1) raise hands first then takbir, (2) simultaneous, (3) takbir first then raise hands. Note the Hanafi scholars who held each view.
The first opinion is attributed to the majority of Hanafi scholars and is declared correct in al-Hidayah.
2
Locate the dalil for each opinion
For each opinion, find the primary hadith cited. Opinion 1 relies on Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi (Bukhari, Sunan). Opinion 2 relies on Wa'il ibn Hujr (Ahmad, Abu Dawud, al-Bayhaqi). Opinion 3 relies on Abu Dawud's narration.
These hadiths are all authentic, but the Hanafi tarjih weighs one over others.
3
Analyze the Hanafi tarjih mechanism
Understand why the majority preferred the first opinion: it is the most authentic (thabita 'an al-nabi) and was practiced by the generality of early scholars. The matter is considered broad, but the first method is most virtuous.
This shows that tarjih does not reject other views outright.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and thamarah al-khilaf
The mufta bihi is the first opinion (raise hands first). The practical difference: a Hanafi follower should raise hands first then say takbir, but if they do it simultaneously or after, the prayer is still valid though less virtuous.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the recommended sequence, not validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا أراد الشروع كبر حاذفاً بعد رفع يديه. هذا أولى من قول صاحب الهداية: «وإذا شرع في الصلاة كبر». فإذا أراد الشروع ... الخ؛ هذا إذا كان إماماً أو منفرداً، فإن كان مقتدياً ينتظر تكبير الإمام، والأفضل أن يكبر بعده، فإن كبر مع تكبيره جاز. كذا في «الذخيرة» و«العون». كبر؛ أي قال: الله أكبر، فلو قال: الله، لا يصير شارعاً في ظاهر الراوية، كما في «الذخيرة»، ويستحب أن يكون آخر التكبير مجزوماً موقوفاً، سواء كان تكبير الافتتاح أو تكبير الانتقال؛ لقول إبراهيم النخعي: «التكبير جزم، والقراءة جزم، والأذان جزم»، أخرجه سعيد بن منصور في «سننه»، والترمذي. بعد رفع يديه؛ هذا أحد الأقوال الثلاثة، وهو أن يرفع يديه أولاً، ثم يُكبر، وصححه في «الهداية»، ونسبه في «المبسوط» إلى عامة مشايخنا، وهو الثابت عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من حديث أبي حميد الساعدي، أخرجه البخاري وأصحاب السنن الأربعة. والقول الثاني ما أشار إليه القدوري واختاره قاضي خان وغيره: إنّه يقارن بين التكبير والرفع، وتوافقه رواية وائل: أنه رأى رسول الله يرفع يديه مع التكبير، أخرجه أحمد وأبو داود والبيهقي. والقول الثالث: إنه يكبر أولاً ثم يرفع يديه، ويشهد له حديث أبي داود: «كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا قام إلى الصلاة رفع يديه حتى يكونا حذو منكبيه، ثم كبر وهما كذلك»، والحقّ أنّ الأمر فيه واسع، وأولى الكيفيات الكيفية الأولى.
When he intends to begin, he says the takbir, dropping the elongation, after raising his hands. This is better than the statement of the author of al-Hidayah: 'When he begins prayer, he says the takbir.' This applies if he is an imam or alone; if he is a follower, he waits for the imam's takbir, and the best is for him to say takbir after him; if he says it with the imam's takbir, it is permissible. As in al-Dhakhirah and al-'Awn. 'He says takbir' means he says 'Allahu akbar'; if he says only 'Allah', he does not become a starter of prayer according to the apparent narration, as in al-Dhakhirah. It is recommended that the end of the takbir be with a stop (jazm), whether it is the opening takbir or the takbir of transition, based on the saying of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: 'The takbir is with a stop, the recitation is with a stop, and the adhan is with a stop.' It was reported by Sa'id ibn Mansur in his Sunan and by al-Tirmidhi. 'After raising his hands': This is one of three opinions, and it is that he raises his hands first, then says takbir. It was declared correct in al-Hidayah, and attributed in al-Mabsut to the generality of our scholars, and it is established from the Prophet (peace be upon him) in the hadith of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi, reported by al-Bukhari and the four Sunan authors. The second opinion is what al-Quduri indicated and Qadi Khan and others chose: that he makes the takbir and the raising simultaneous, supported by the narration of Wa'il that he saw the Messenger of Allah raising his hands with the takbir, reported by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and al-Bayhaqi. The third opinion: that he says takbir first then raises his hands, supported by the hadith of Abu Dawud: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) when he stood for prayer, raised his hands until they were level with his shoulders, then said takbir while they were like that.' The truth is that the matter is broad, and the best of the manners is the first one.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi scholarly discussion on the timing of hand-raising relative to the opening takbir in salah. The commentator begins by contrasting the formulation here with that of al-Hidayah. He notes that the ruling differs for the follower: a follower should wait for the imam's takbir and preferably recite after him, though reciting simultaneously is permissible. The takbir must be 'Allahu akbar'; merely saying 'Allah' does not constitute entering prayer. The takbir should be pronounced with a stop (jazm) at the end, not with elongation, following the practice of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i. The main discussion presents three opinions on the sequence of hand-raising and takbir. The first opinion, which is the preferred Hanafi position, is to raise the hands first and then say takbir. This is supported by the hadith of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi and is declared correct by al-Hidayah and attributed to the majority of Hanafi scholars by al-Mabsut. The second opinion, favoured by al-Quduri and Qadi Khan and also narrated from Abu Yusuf, is to raise the hands simultaneously with the takbir, based on the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr. The third opinion, to say takbir first then raise hands, is supported by a hadith from Abu Dawud. The commentator concludes that the matter is flexible (waasi'), but the first method is most virtuous.

Key Terms
التكبير (al-takbir): The phrase 'Allahu akbar' said at specific points in prayer, particularly the opening takbir (takbirat al-ihram).
الجزم (al-jazm): Pronouncing the final letter of the takbir with a stop (sukoon), without elongation or vowelization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school regarding the timing of hand-raising with the opening takbir?
A) Raise hands and say takbir simultaneously
B) Raise hands first, then say takbir
C) Say takbir first, then raise hands
D) It does not matter; all are equally preferred

Key Takeaway
Raise hands first, then say takbir – this is the preferred Hanafi method, though all three sequences are valid.

Pronunciation and Finger Position in Takbir
Ruling on avoiding elongation and proper finger position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vowel elongation in the takbir, distinguish between elongation in 'Allah' and 'Akbar', and apply the sunnah finger position.

Core Idea
Elongation in 'Akbar' invalidates prayer; natural finger position is sunnah.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains two essential aspects of the opening takbir: proper pronunciation and natural hand position. While many worshippers focus solely on raising the hands, the Hanafi school emphasizes that the articulation of the takbir can affect the validity of the prayer. The distinction between elongating 'Allah' and 'Akbar' has practical consequences for every Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage gives two separate rulings: (1) avoiding elongation (al-hadhf) in the takbir, and (2) the natural position of the fingers.
The first part is the main focus, the second is a sunnah.
2
Analyze the elongation in 'Allah'
Elongation at the beginning, middle, or end of 'Allah' is a mistake (khatā') but does not invalidate the prayer. Some scholars said it suggests disbelief if deliberate.
Intra-Hanafi difference exists.
3
Analyze the elongation in 'Akbar'
Any elongation in 'Akbar' invalidates the prayer because it changes the word to 'al-Ikbar' (name of a devil). Deliberate elongation in the middle raises fear of kufr.
This is a clear invalidator (mubtil).
4
Examine the finger position ruling
The hadith from Ibn Hibban states that the Prophet spread his fingers naturally. The sunnah is to leave them in their natural state, neither forced spread nor clenched.
This is a sunnah, not a condition of validity.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that elongation in 'Akbar' invalidates; in 'Allah' it is makruh but does not invalidate. Natural finger position is sunnah.
Based on later Hanafi works like Jami' al-Mudmarat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير مفرج أصابعه ولا ضام. المراد بالحذف: أن لا يأتي بالمد في همزة الله، ولا في باء أكبر غير مفرج أصابعه ولا ضام بل يتركها على حالها. قال بعض مشايخنا: لا يأتي بالمد؛ فإنّ المدّ إن كان في لفظ: «الله»، فإن كان في أوله كان خطأ، ولكن لا تفسد به الصلاة، وقال بعض مشايخنا: يوهم الكفر، وإن كان في وسطه أو في آخره فكذلك هو خطأ، لكن لا تفسد به الصلاة، وإن كان في لفظ: «أكبر» تفسد به الصلاة سواء كان في أوّله أو أوسطه أو آخره، بل لو تعمده في وسطه خيف عليه الكفر؛ لأنَّ الإكبار اسم للشيطان. كذا في «جامع المضمرات». وذكر في «البناية» و«البحر» و«الحَلْبة»: إنّ المد في أول لفظ: «الله» لا يصير به شارعاً في الصلاة، ولو تعمد خيف عليه الكفر. غير مفرّج من التفريج؛ أي لا يتكلف عند رفع اليدين في تفريج الأصابع، ولا في ضمها بل يتركها على حالها وهو المراد بما أخرجه ابن حبان عن أبي هريرة: «إن رسول الله كان ينشر أصابعه في الصلاة نشرا».
He does not spread his fingers nor clench them. The meaning of 'dropping the elongation' (al-hadhf) is that he does not produce the elongation in the hamzah of 'Allah' nor in the ba of 'Akbar', and he does not spread his fingers nor clench them, but leaves them as they are. Some of our scholars said: He should not produce elongation; as for elongation in the word 'Allah', if it is at its beginning, it is wrong but does not invalidate the prayer; and some of our scholars said it suggests disbelief. If it is in the middle or end, similarly it is wrong but does not invalidate the prayer. As for elongation in the word 'Akbar', it invalidates the prayer whether at its beginning, middle, or end. In fact, if he deliberately elongates in the middle, it is feared that he may fall into disbelief, because 'al-Ikbar' is a name of a devil. This is in Jami' al-Mudmarat. And it is mentioned in al-Binayah, al-Bahr, and al-Halbah: Elongation at the beginning of the word 'Allah' does not make one a starter of the prayer, and if deliberate, it is feared he may fall into disbelief. 'He does not spread' (ghayru mufarrij) from tafrij; i.e., he does not exert himself when raising the hands in spreading the fingers nor in clenching them, but leaves them as they are. This is intended by what Ibn Hibban reported from Abu Hurayrah: 'Indeed, the Messenger of Allah would spread his fingers in prayer naturally.'
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the manner of pronouncing the takbir and the position of the fingers. The term 'al-hadhf' (dropping elongation) means to avoid prolonging the vowel in the hamzah of 'Allah' and the ba of 'Akbar'. The commentator explains that if a person elongates the first letter of 'Allah', it is a mistake but does not invalidate the prayer. Some scholars said it even suggests disbelief (kufr) if done deliberately. Elongation in the middle or end of 'Allah' is also wrong but not invalidating. However, elongation in 'Akbar' nullifies the prayer regardless of position because 'al-Ikbar' is a name of a devil. If done deliberately in the middle, it risks disbelief. The texts also rule that such elongation prevents one from entering the state of prayer (shuru'). Regarding the fingers, the sunnah is to neither deliberately spread nor clench them but to leave them in their natural, relaxed state. This is supported by the hadith of Abu Hurayrah that the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) would spread his fingers naturally during prayer.

Key Terms
الحذف (al-hadhf): Leaving out the elongation (mad) in the pronunciation of the takbir, specifically in the hamzah of 'Allah' and the ba of 'Akbar'.
المد (al-madd): Elongation of vowels in the words of the takbir, which is discouraged or invalidating depending on placement.
الإكبار (al-ikbar): A name of a devil; pronouncing 'Akbar' with elongation in the middle resembles this word, risking kufr if deliberate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is makruh but the prayer is valid
B) It invalidates the prayer
C) It is recommended
D) It is obligatory

Key Takeaway
Elongation in 'Akbar' invalidates the prayer; natural finger position is sunnah.

Three Views on Takbir and Hand Raising Sequence
Discusses three scholarly views on sequence of takbir and hand raising

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi views on the sequence of takbir and hand raising, identify their hadith evidence, and explain why all are valid with a preferred practice.

Core Idea
All three sequences are valid, but raising hands first is the preferred Hanafi practice.

When starting prayer, the Hanafi school offers flexibility on whether to raise the hands before, during, or after saying the opening takbir. This lesson explores three views from within the school, each supported by hadith, and clarifies why all are acceptable while noting the preferred method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The question is the sequence of two actions: raising the hands and saying 'Allahu Akbar' at the start of prayer. Determine what is at stake — it is a matter of preference, not validity.
2
List the three views
View 1: raise hands first, then say takbir. View 2: synchronize both. View 3: say takbir first, then raise hands. Note that the first view is implied as the baseline.
The author does not explicitly state the first view but refers to it as 'the first manner'.
3
Examine the evidence for each view
View 2 is supported by the narration of Wail (Ahmad, Abu Dawud, al-Bayhaqi). View 3 is supported by the hadith of Abu Dawud. View 1 has general practice but is not directly cited here.
All evidences are ahad hadith, so none are qat'i.
4
Understand the author's conclusion
The author states 'al-haqq anna al-amr fihi wasi'' (the matter is broad) and then says the first manner is most preferable. This shows acceptance of all views with a tarjih for the first.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is in the sequence performed; all are valid. The preferred method yields follow of stronger evidence (the first manner).
This flexibility is typical for sunnah actions.

The second view, attributed to al-Quduri and chosen by Qadikhan, is to synchronize the raising with the takbir. The third view is to say the takbir first and then raise the hands. Each view has hadith support. The author concludes that the matter is flexible, but the first manner is preferable. This is a typical Hanafi approach of allowing flexibility in non-obligatory actions while recommending the best practice.

Key Terms

الرفع (al-raf'): Raising the hands during the opening takbir

Ruling on Elongation in the Takbir Words
Explains the effect of elongating 'Allah' or 'Akbar' on prayer validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between elongation in 'Allah' and 'Akbar' and state whether the prayer is invalidated.

Core Idea
Elongation in 'Akbar' invalidates the prayer because it changes the meaning to a devil's name; elongation in 'Allah' does not invalidate.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the precise rules for pronouncing the takbir. The matn instructs to avoid elongation. The commentary clarifies that the consequence depends on which word is elongated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the mistake
The act is the opening takbir of prayer. The mistake is elongating the vowel sound (madd) in either 'Allah' or 'Akbar'.
2
Note the two words affected
The passage examines elongation in the word 'Allah' (hamza) and the word 'Akbar' (ba').
3
Determine ruling for 'Allah'
Elongation in 'Allah' (beginning, middle, or end) is a mistake (khat'') but does not invalidate the prayer. Some shaykhs warned it suggests disbelief, but the relied-upon view is that it is not invalidating.
4
Determine ruling for 'Akbar'
Elongation in 'Akbar' invalidates the prayer, regardless of position. If intentional in the middle, disbelief is feared because the word becomes a name of the devil (al-Ikbar).
5
Understand the reason (talil)
The change in meaning to 'al-Ikbar' (a devil's name) is the cause for invalidation. This is a linguistic and legal reasoning that links the error to the nullification of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد بالحذف : أن لا يأتي بالمد في همزة الله ، ولا في باء أكبر غير مفرج أصابعه ولا ضام) بل يتركها على حالها. [١] قوله : : لا يأتي بالمد ؛ فإنّ المدّ إن كان في لفظ : «الله»، فإن كان في أوله كان خطأ، ولكن لا تفسد به الصلاة، وقال بعض مشايخنا: يوهم الكفر، وإن كان في وسطه أو في آخره فكذلك هو خطأ، لكن لا تفسد به الصلاة، وإن كان في لفظ : «أكبر» تفسد به الصلاة سواء كان في أوّله أو أوسطه أو آخره ، بل لو تعمده في وسطه خيف عليه الكفر ؛ لأنَّ الإكبار اسم للشيطان. كذا في «جامع المضمرات». وذكر في «البناية» و«البحر» و«الحَلْبة»: إنّ المد في أول لفظ : «الله» لا يصير به شارعاً في الصلاة، ولو تعمد خيف عليه الكفر.
The meaning of al-hadhf (shortening) is that he should not elongate the hamza of Allah nor the ba of Akbar, and not spreading his fingers nor closing them, but leave them as they are. [1] His saying: 'do not elongate'; because elongation, if it is in the word 'Allah', if it is at its beginning, it is a mistake but it does not invalidate the prayer, and some of our shaykhs said: it suggests disbelief, and if it is in its middle or end, similarly it is a mistake but does not invalidate the prayer. And if it is in the word 'Akbar', it invalidates the prayer whether at its beginning, middle, or end. Rather, if he intentionally elongates in its middle, it is feared for him disbelief, because 'al-Ikbar' (elongation in Akbar) is a name for the devil. Thus in Jami' al-Mudmarat. And it is mentioned in al-Binaya, al-Bahr, and al-Halba: that elongation at the beginning of the word 'Allah' does not make him enter into the prayer, and if intentional, disbelief is feared.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling on prolonging the vowels (mad) in the opening takbir. The matn instructs not to elongate the hamza of Allah or the ba of Akbar. The commentary explains that elongation in 'Allah' is a mistake but does not nullify the prayer, although some scholars warn it may suggest disbelief. However, elongation in 'Akbar' (especially in the middle) invalidates the prayer and may lead to disbelief because it changes the meaning to a name of the devil. The later sources like al-Binaya add that elongation at the beginning of 'Allah' prevents one from being considered as having started the prayer. This shows the precision Hanafi fiqh gives to the wording of the takbir.

Key Terms
المد (al-madd): Prolonging the vowel sound (elongation) in the pronunciation of a letter.
الإكبار (al-ikbar): A name for the devil, which results from mispronouncing 'Akbar' with elongation in the middle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Elongation in 'Akbar' invalidates the prayer due to change of meaning; elongation in 'Allah' does not, but should be avoided.

Ruling on Finger Position When Raising Hands in Prayer
Explains fingers should be neither spread nor clenched, but left natural

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah position of the fingers during the opening takbir and identify the evidence from hadith.

Core Idea
In takbir, fingers are left naturally – neither spread nor clenched – following the Prophet's practice.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the commentator clarifies the sunnah regarding finger position when raising the hands for takbir. It explains that the natural state is recommended, supported by a hadith from Abu Hurairah. Ali al-Qari further specifies when spreading or clenching is desirable elsewhere in the prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The finger position when raising hands for takbir. The ruling is sunnah – not wajib.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith cited from Ibn Hibban: 'The Messenger of Allah used to spread his fingers in prayer as a spreading.' This is the primary proof.
The hadith is ahad, so it gives zanni proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'nashr' (spreading) is interpreted as natural, not exaggerated, based on the context and the companion's explanation. Thus it supports the natural state.
The literal spreading is understood as moderate, not forced.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools may recommend deliberate spreading; but the Hanafi school adopts a middle path.
Ali al-Qari distinguishes between positions in ruku' and sujud.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position is to leave fingers as they are, as stated by Ali al-Qari and the matn.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Touching Earlobes in Salah: Means or Sunnah?
Clarifies that touching earlobes is not an independent sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an independent sunnah and a means of alignment, and state the Hanafi position on touching earlobes when raising hands.

Core Idea
Touching earlobes is not a separate sunnah; it is a practical method to achieve correct hand alignment.

In the Hanafi school, raising the hands to the earlobes during the opening takbir is a sunnah. Some later scholars mentioned touching the earlobes with the thumbs. This lesson clarifies the status of that action: is it an independent sunnah or merely a means? We will examine the evidence and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action and the dispute
The passage discusses the statement 'massa bi ibhamayhi shahmatay udhunayhi' (touching with his thumbs his earlobes). Determine whether this touching is an independent sunnah or a means to achieve alignment.
The matn mentions touching, but the commentary immediately clarifies its status.
2
Locate the evidence
Find the hadith cited: raising hands to the level of the ears (from multiple sahabah) and to the level of the shoulders. Note that there is no explicit hadith for touching the earlobes.
The author states there is no evidence (la daleel alayhi fi riwayah).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
How do the hadith prove the sunnah of raising hands to alignment? They show the Prophet (ﷺ) raising until his thumbs were near or level with the earlobes. The touching is inferred from the proximity, not directly commanded.
The hadith of Anas and al-Bara' mention 'muhadhat' (alignment), not 'mass' (touching).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some later Hanafi scholars (Qadikhan, author of Mukhtarat al-Nawazil, al-Zahiriyyah) included touching in their texts. The author follows them but notes it is not an independent sunnah.
The reason for their inclusion is to achieve alignment and dispel waswas (whispers).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Ali al-Qari reconciles the different hadith (ears vs. shoulders) by interpreting them for different occasions. The relied-upon position is that raising to alignment is sunnah; touching is permissible as a means, not an act of worship.
This is a practical flexibility, not a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ماساً بإبهاميهِ شَحْمَتي أذنيه. [ ۱ ] قوله : ماساً ؛ أي حال كونه لا مساً بطرفي إبهاميه شحمتي أذنيه ؛ أي ما لان من أسفل الأذن، وهذا لا ذكر له في «الهداية»، وإنما فيه أن يرفع يديه حتى يحاذي بإبهاميه شحمتي أذنيه، وهكذا ذكره كثير من مشايخنا. وذكر قاضي خان في «فتاواه»، وصاحب «الهداية» في «مختارات النوازل»، وصاحب «الظهيرية»: المس، وتبعهم المصنّف ، وهو ليس بسنّة مستقلة، فإنه لا دليل عليه في رواية. ولعل من استحبه إنّما استحبه تحقيقاً للمحاذاة ، ودفعاً للوسوسة، والثابت عن النبي هو الرفع إلى محاذاة الأذنين فحسب (۲) ، أخرجه أبو داود والنسائي والحاكم والدارقطني والطحاوي ومسلم، والطبراني، وأحمد واسحاق بن راهويه بروايات وائل وأنس والبراء ومالك بن الحويرث. وثبت عنه الرفع إلى محاذاة المنكبين (۳) أيضاً عند أصحاب السنن الأربعة و مسلم والطحاوي وغيرهم، والكلّ صحيح ثابت ومحمول على اختلاف الأوقات، كما ذكره علي القاري في «سند الأنام شرح مسند الإمام»(4). (۲) فعن أنس قال : رأيت رسول الله اكبر فحاذى بإبهاميه اليسرى ، ثم ركع حتى استقر كل مفصل منه وانحط بالتكبير حتى سبقت ركبتاه يديه» في «المستدرك»(١ : ٣٤٩)، وصححه، و مسند الروياني»(۱ : ۲۳۹)، وغيرها. وعن البراء بن عازب الله له قال : كان النبي لا اله إذا كبر لافتتاح الصلاة رفع يديه حتى يكون إبهاماه قريبا من شحمتي أذنيه» في «شرح معاني الآثار» (١) : ١٩٦)، وغيره. (۳) فعن ابن عمر قال : كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم اذا قام للصلاة رفع يديه حتى تكونا بحذو منكبيه ثم كبر في (صحيح ابن خزيمة»(۱ : ۲۳۲)، وغيره. (٤) سند الأنام»(ص٤٩٣).
Touching with his thumbs the lobes of his ears. [1] His saying: 'massa' (touching); meaning in the state of touching with the tips of his thumbs the lobes of his ears, i.e., the soft part at the bottom of the ear. This is not mentioned in al-Hidayah; rather it says to raise his hands until his thumbs are level with his earlobes. Many of our shaykhs mentioned that. Qadikhan in his Fatawa, the author of al-Hidayah in Mukhtarat al-Nawazil, and the author of al-Zahiriyyah mentioned the touching, and the author followed them. But it is not an independent sunnah, for there is no evidence for it in narrations. Perhaps those who recommended it did so to achieve alignment and to dispel whispers. What is established from the Prophet is raising to the level of the ears only (2), reported by Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, al-Hakim, al-Daraqutni, al-Tahawi, Muslim, al-Tabarani, Ahmad and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh through narrations of Wail, Anas, al-Bara', and Malik ibn al-Huwayrith. And it is also established to raise to the level of the shoulders (3) from the authors of the four Sunans, Muslim, al-Tahawi and others. All are authentic and established and are interpreted as for different occasions, as mentioned by Ali al-Qari in 'Sund al-Anam Sharh Musnad al-Imam'. (2) From Anas: 'I saw the Messenger of Allah...' (3) From Ibn Umar: 'When the Messenger of Allah stood for prayer he raised his hands until they were level with his shoulders...' (4) Sund al-Anam (p. 493).
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage discusses the practice of touching the earlobes with the thumbs during the opening takbir. The matn states 'massa bi ibhamayhi shahmatay udhunayhi' (touching with his thumbs his earlobes). The commentary clarifies that this is not found in al-Hidayah, but some later Hanafi scholars like Qadikhan mentioned it. However, it is not an independent sunnah because there is no clear hadith for it; rather it is a means to achieve proper alignment of the hands with the ears. The established sunnah is to raise the hands until they are level with the earlobes or the shoulders. Both narrations are authentic and are applied flexibly based on different times or situations. Ali al-Qari reconciles them by suggesting they correspond to different occasions.

Key Terms
شحمة الأذن (shahmat al-udhun): The lobe of the ear, the soft lower part
المحاذاة (al-muhadhat): Alignment; making the hands level with the ears or shoulders
وسوسة (waswasah): Whisper from Shaytan that causes doubt in worship

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why did some scholars recommend touching the earlobes with the thumbs?
A) It is a proven sunnah from the Prophet
B) To achieve proper alignment and avoid whispers
C) It is required for the prayer to be valid
D) It is a distinguishing sign of the Hanafi school

Key Takeaway
Touching the earlobes is not an independent sunnah; it is a permissible means to achieve the established sunnah of hand alignment in salah.

Raising Hands at the Opening Takbir: Manner and Gender Considerations
Commentary on hand raising with proof and gender ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on raising hands at the opening takbir, differentiate between the sunnah and non-sunnah practices, and apply the preferred position for women.

Core Idea
Raising hands to the ears is sunnah; touching earlobes is not an independent practice, only for alignment.

This passage from the commentary clarifies the proper manner of raising hands (raf' al-yadayn) at the beginning of prayer. While many Hanafi texts mention touching the earlobes, the commentator emphasises that the authentic practice is simply raising until the thumbs are level with the ears. A separate ruling for women prioritises modesty, recommending shoulder-level raising.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is raising hands at the opening takbir. It is a sunnah muakkadah (confirmed sunnah), not wajib.
The ruling is based on multiple authentic hadiths.
2
Distinguish the established sunnah from later practice
The text clarifies that touching the earlobes (al-mass) is not an independent sunnah; it was introduced only to achieve alignment or repel waswas.
The reliable position (zahir riwayah) is simply raising to the ears.
3
Note the dual authentic narrations
Both raising to the ears and to the shoulders are authentically reported from the Prophet (peace be upon him), interpreted as different occasions.
This reconciles the different practices.
4
Apply the ruling for women
The mufta bihi view is that a woman raises her hands to her shoulders for modesty, although there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement (Abu Hanifa, in one narration, said she raises like a man).
Qadi Khan and the commentator prefer the shoulder level.
5
Inter-madhhab comparison
Hanafis prefer raising to the ears; Shafi'is prefer raising to the shoulders; both are valid and not mutually condemnatory.
This shows the breadth of legitimate practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ماساً بإبهاميهِ شَحْمَتي أذنيه (ماساً بإبهاميهِ شَحْمَتي أذنيه وقال علي القاري له في شرح المشكاة»: «لا يندب التفريج إلا في حالة وضع الراحتين على الركبتين، ولا الضمّ إلا في حال السجود، وفيما سواهما يترك على ما عليه العادة» (۱). [ ۱ ] قوله : ماساً ؛ أي حال كونه لا مساً بطرفي إبهاميه شحمتي أذنيه ؛ أي ما لان من أسفل الأذن، وهذا لا ذكر له في «الهداية»، وإنما فيه أن يرفع يديه حتى يحاذي بإبهاميه شحمتي أذنيه، وهكذا ذكره كثير من مشايخنا. وذكر قاضي خان في «فتاواه»، وصاحب «الهداية» في «مختارات النوازل»، وصاحب «الظهيرية»: المس، وتبعهم المصنّف ، وهو ليس بسنّة مستقلة، فإنه لا دليل عليه في رواية. ولعل من استحبه إنّما استحبه تحقيقاً للمحاذاة ، ودفعاً للوسوسة، والثابت عن النبي هو الرفع إلى محاذاة الأذنين فحسب (۲) ، أخرجه أبو داود والنسائي والحاكم والدارقطني والطحاوي ومسلم، والطبراني، وأحمد واسحاق بن راهويه بروايات وائل وأنس والبراء ومالك بن الحويرث. وثبت عنه الرفع إلى محاذاة المنكبين (۳) أيضاً عند أصحاب السنن الأربعة و مسلم والطحاوي وغيرهم، والكلّ صحيح ثابت ومحمول على اختلاف الأوقات، كما ذكره علي القاري في «سند الأنام شرح مسند الإمام»(4). والمرأة ترفع حذاءَ منكبيها، فإن بدل التكبير بالله أجل، أو أعظم، أو الرحمن أكبر، أو لا إله إلا الله، أو بالفارسية (4) [2]. [1] قوله : والمرأة ؛ سواء كانت حرَّة أو أمةً على الأصح ؛ وقيل : الأمة كالرجل. (1) كذا في «البحر»، وغيره. [٢] قوله : حذاء ؛ بكسر الحاء المهملة ؛ أي مقابل منكبيها، قال قاضي خان في «فتاواه»: «المرأة ترفع كما يرفعُ الرجل في روايةِ الحَسَن عن أبي حنيفة ، وقال (۳) : ترفع المرأة حذاء منكبيها، ويروى في ذلك حديثاً(۳)، مقاتل الرازي بن وهو أقرب إلى الستر» (1). [3] قوله : أو الرحمن ؛ وكذا بكلّ صفةٍ لا تطلقُ إلا على الله ، كالخالق والرزاق، وإن أطلق على غير الله ، أو لم يوجد مثله في القرآن، أو أشبه كلام الناس، كالرحيم والحكيم والكريم، لا يصير شارعاً به. كذا في «البزازية). [٤]قوله : أو بالفارسية ؛ بكسر الراء المهملة ، يعني اللسان المنسوبة إلى الفارس ، فالأمر فيه واسع، وقد اختار أصحاب الشافعي الكيفية الثانية، وأصحابنا الأولى من غير إنكار الأخرى، وتحقيق هذه المباحث بل والمباحث السابقة والآتية ليطلب من «السعاية»، فإن فيه لطالب الإنصاف كفاية.
Touching his earlobes with his thumbs: 'Touching his earlobes with his thumbs.' And Ali al-Qari said in his commentary on Mishkat: 'Spreading the fingers is not recommended except when placing the palms on the knees, nor clasping except in prostration; in other cases, it is left as customary.' [1] His saying: 'touching' – meaning while touching the lobes of his ears with the tips of his thumbs, i.e., the soft part of the lower ear. This is not mentioned in Al-Hidayah; rather, it states that he raises his hands until his thumbs are level with his earlobes, and many of our scholars mentioned this. Qadi Khan in his Fatawa, the author of Al-Hidayah in Mukhtarat al-Nawazil, and the author of Al-Zahiriyyah mentioned touching, and the author followed them, but this is not an independent sunnah, for there is no evidence for it in narration. Perhaps whoever deemed it recommended did so to achieve alignment and repel waswas. What is established from the Prophet is raising to the level of the ears only, as narrated by Abu Dawud, al-Nasa'i, al-Hakim, al-Daraqutni, al-Tahawi, Muslim, al-Tabarani, Ahmad, and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh through the narrations of Wa'il, Anas, al-Bara', and Malik ibn al-Huwayrith. It is also established that he raised to the level of the shoulders from the authors of the four Sunan, Muslim, al-Tahawi, and others; all are authentic and established, and are interpreted as different occasions, as Ali al-Qari mentioned in Sind al-Anam. The woman raises level with her shoulders. If he substitutes the takbir with 'Allahu Ajal', 'Allahu A'zam', 'Al-Rahmanu Akbar', 'La ilaha illallah', or in Persian. [1] His saying: 'the woman' – whether free or slave, according to the most correct view; it is said: the slave is like a man. [2] His saying: 'level with' – i.e., opposite her shoulders. Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: 'The woman raises as the man raises according to the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifah, and he (Muhammad) said: the woman raises level with her shoulders, and a hadith is narrated about that by Muqatil al-Razi, and it is closer to modesty.' [3] His saying: 'or Al-Rahman' – and likewise any attribute that is only used for Allah, like al-Khaliq and al-Razzaq. If it is also used for others, or does not occur in the Quran, or resembles human speech, like al-Rahim, al-Hakim, al-Karim, one does not become a starter of prayer with it. This is from al-Bazzaziyyah. [4] His saying: 'or in Persian' – meaning the language attributed to Persia. The matter is wide in scope. The Shafi'is chose the second manner, and our companions the first, without denouncing the other. The investigation of these discussions, and previous and upcoming ones, should be sought from Al-Si'ayah, for it suffices the seeker of fairness.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the section of prayer description (Sifat al-Salah) from the matn. It addresses two connected rulings: the manner of raising hands (raf' al-yadayn) at the opening takbir, and the valid wording of the takbir itself. The commentator begins by clarifying the phrase 'touching his earlobes with his thumbs' from the matn. He notes that while some Hanafi authorities mention touching (al-mass), this is not an independent sunnah; rather, the established practice from the Prophet (peace be upon him) is to raise the hands until the thumbs are level with the earlobes. Both raising to the ears and raising to the shoulders are reported authentically, and the differences are attributed to varying occasions. For women, the commentator explains the preferred position: she raises her hands to shoulder level, based on a narration from Muhammad al-Shaybani that promotes modesty. There is an intra-Hanafi disagreement: in one narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad, Abu Hanifah held that the woman raises like a man. The text then turns to the wording of takbir. If one substitutes 'Allahu Akbar' with 'Allahu Ajal' (Allah is Greater), 'Allahu A'zam' (Allah is Mightiest), 'Al-Rahmanu Akbar' (The Most Merciful is Greater), or 'La ilaha illallah' (There is no god but Allah), or says the takbir in Persian, it is valid. However, using attributes that are not exclusive to Allah (like al-Rahim, al-Hakim, al-Karim) does not constitute a valid takbir because they resemble human speech. The commentator concludes by noting the flexibility in this matter, and the inter-madhhab difference: Shafi'is prefer raising to the shoulders, Hanafis prefer raising to the ears, without mutual condemnation.

Key Terms
شحمة الأذن (shahmat al-udhun): The soft, fleshy lower part of the ear (earlobe).
رفع اليدين (raf' al-yadayn): Raising the hands at the opening takbir of prayer.
تكبيرة الإحرام (takbir al-tahrimah): The opening 'Allahu Akbar' that begins the prayer; it is a pillar (fard).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view, to where does a woman raise her hands at the opening takbir?
A) To her earlobes, like a man.
B) To her shoulders, for modesty.
C) To her chest level.
D) She does not raise her hands at all.

Key Takeaway
Raising hands to the ears is the sunnah for men; for women, the mufta bihi is raising to the shoulders for modesty.

Valid Wording for the Opening Takbir in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on valid substitutes for the opening takbir wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid substitute wordings for the opening takbir and explain the Hanafi principle of using exclusive divine attributes.

Core Idea
The takbir can use any exclusive attribute of Allah or Persian; non-exclusive attributes like al-Rahim are invalid.

This passage addresses the wording of the opening takbir (takbir al-tahrimah). While the standard phrase is 'Allahu Akbar', the commentator explains that other phrases using exclusive attributes of Allah are also valid. The flexibility reflects the principle that the core of takbir is declaring Allah's greatness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on the takbir
The opening takbir is a pillar (rukn) of prayer. The standard wording is 'Allahu Akbar'.
Without the takbir, the prayer is not validly begun.
2
List the valid substitute wordings
The text mentions: 'Allahu Ajal', 'Allahu A'zam', 'Al-Rahmanu Akbar', 'La ilaha illallah', or any exclusive attribute like al-Khaliq, al-Razzaq.
These are all phrases that exclusively describe Allah.
3
Identify invalid substitutes
Attributes that are also used for others (e.g., al-Rahim, al-Hakim, al-Karim) or phrases that do not occur in the Quran or resemble human speech are invalid.
Using such phrases does not count as starting the prayer.
4
Note the ruling for Persian
Saying the takbir in Persian is valid; the matter is wide in scope.
This shows the Hanafi leniency for non-Arabic speakers.
5
Note the inter-madhhab difference
Shafi'is require the specific Arabic 'Allahu Akbar'; Hanafis allow substitutes.
Both positions are based on principles, and the text notes mutual acceptance.

Source Text

Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: 'The woman raises as the man raises according to the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifah, and he (Muhammad) said: the woman raises level with her shoulders, and a hadith is narrated about that by Muqatil al-Razi, and it is closer to modesty.' [3] His saying: 'or Al-Rahman' – and likewise any attribute that is only used for Allah, like al-Khaliq and al-Razzaq. If it is also used for others, or does not occur in the Quran, or resembles human speech, like al-Rahim, al-Hakim, al-Karim, one does not become a starter of prayer with it. This is from al-Bazzaziyyah. [4] His saying: 'or in Persian' – meaning the language attributed to Persia. The matter is wide in scope. The Shafi'is chose the second manner, and our companions the first, without denouncing the other. The investigation of these discussions, and previous and upcoming ones, should be sought from Al-Si'ayah, for it suffices the seeker of fairness.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the section of prayer description (Sifat al-Salah) from the matn. It addresses two connected rulings: the manner of raising hands (raf' al-yadayn) at the opening takbir, and the valid wording of the takbir itself. The commentator begins by clarifying the phrase 'touching his earlobes with his thumbs' from the matn. He notes that while some Hanafi authorities mention touching (al-mass), this is not an independent sunnah; rather, the established practice from the Prophet (peace be upon him) is to raise the hands until the thumbs are level with the earlobes. Both raising to the ears and raising to the shoulders are reported authentically, and the differences are attributed to varying occasions. For women, the commentator explains the preferred position: she raises her hands to shoulder level, based on a narration from Muhammad al-Shaybani that promotes modesty. There is an intra-Hanafi disagreement: in one narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad, Abu Hanifah held that the woman raises like a man. The text then turns to the wording of takbir. If one substitutes 'Allahu Akbar' with 'Allahu Ajal' (Allah is Greater), 'Allahu A'zam' (Allah is Mightiest), 'Al-Rahmanu Akbar' (The Most Merciful is Greater), or 'La ilaha illallah' (There is no god but Allah), or says the takbir in Persian, it is valid. However, using attributes that are not exclusive to Allah (like al-Rahim, al-Hakim, al-Karim) does not constitute a valid takbir because they resemble human speech. The commentator concludes by noting the flexibility in this matter, and the inter-madhhab difference: Shafi'is prefer raising to the shoulders, Hanafis prefer raising to the ears, without mutual condemnation.

Key Terms
شحمة الأذن (shahmat al-udhun): The soft, fleshy lower part of the ear (earlobe).
رفع اليدين (raf' al-yadayn): Raising the hands at the opening takbir of prayer.
تكبيرة الإحرام (takbir al-tahrimah): The opening 'Allahu Akbar' that begins the prayer; it is a pillar (fard).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a valid substitute for the opening takbir according to the passage?
A) 'Allahu A'zam'
B) 'Al-Rahmanu Akbar'
C) 'Al-Rahimu Akbar'
D) 'Allahu Ajal'

Key Takeaway
The opening takbir can be said using any exclusive divine attribute, 'La ilaha illallah', or Persian; non-exclusive attributes and human-like phrases are invalid.

Woman's Hand Position: Shoulders or Ears?
Explains the woman's hand position and the intra-Hanafi difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi mufta bihi position on a woman's hand raising during the opening takbir and explain the intra-Hanafi difference with its basis.

Core Idea
A woman raises her hands to her shoulders—the mufta bihi—though some narrations allow raising to the ears.

The position of a woman's hands during the opening takbir is a sunnah of prayer, but there is a nuance within the Hanafi school. While the relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is that she raises her hands to shoulder level, a transmission from Abu Hanifa via Hasan ibn Ziyad permits raising to the ears like a man. This lesson unpacks the evidence and the school's preference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the matn (al-Wiqayah) says: the woman raises her hands parallel to her shoulders. This is the position in Zahir al-Riwayah.
The text uses حذاء منكبيها.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Who disagrees within the school? Hasan ibn Ziyad narrates from Abu Hanifa that the woman raises like a man (to the ears). The Shafi‘i school also prefers the second manner (raising to ears).
‘Opponent’ here refers to intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab views.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is their dalil? Hasan’s narration is based on a hadith (though not quoted), and the Shafi‘is rely on general hadith about raising hands to the ears.
The text only mentions that a hadith is narrated, not its text.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school respond? They do not reject the other view; both are considered valid (فالأمر فيه واسع). The preference for shoulders is based on modesty (أقرب إلى الستر) and Qadi Khan’s fatwa.
The school’s response is one of tolerance, not refutation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? A woman may raise to shoulders (preferred) or to ears (permissible). The mufta bihi is shoulders.
In practice, both are acceptable, but the fatwa follows the majority.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling regarding the positioning of a woman's hands during the opening takbir in prayer. The primary ruling (from the matn) is that a woman should raise her hands to the level of her shoulders. The commentary clarifies that this ruling applies to both free women and slave women, though there is a minority view that a slave woman is like a man. The text then notes an important intra-Hanafi difference: in the transmission of Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa, the woman raises her hands as a man does (to the ears), but the position preferred by Qadi Khan and the majority of the school is that she raises to the shoulders. A hadith is cited in support, and the reasoning given is that this is 'closer to modesty.' The Shafi'is are noted as preferring the second manner (raising to the ears), but the Hanafis do not condemn either practice; there is room for both. This passage thus establishes the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position as the hands to the shoulders, while acknowledging a valid alternative transmission.

Key Terms
حذاء (hidhā'): Parallel to, opposite; here it indicates the height at which the woman places her hands relative to her shoulders.
منكبيها (mankibayhā): Her shoulders; dual form of mankib, indicating both shoulders.

Key Takeaway

Reciting Takbir and Quran in Non-Arabic Languages: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on non-Arabic recitation and takbīr substitution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on reciting the Quran in a non-Arabic language and explain the reasoning behind each position, including the retraction claim and the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, reciting the Quran in a non-Arabic language is permissible for all per Abu Hanifa but only for the unable per his two companions.

This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āya clarifies a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of reciting the Quran and takbīr in non-Arabic languages. The issue hinges on whether the meaning of the Quran is sufficient or the Arabic wording is integral. Abu Hanīfa’s position, based on the primacy of meaning, is contrasted with the Ṣāḥibayn’s restriction for those unable to recite Arabic. The text also examines the claim of retraction by Abu Hanīfa and its authenticity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their initial ruling
Understand that the passage first discusses valid substitution of takbīr with exclusive divine attributes (e.g., al-Khāliq), then moves to the main dispute on non-Arabic recitation.
The preliminary point ensures that only attributes uniquely used for Allāh can replace takbīr; otherwise, it does not count.
2
State the Hanafi positions
Abū Ḥanīfa: recitation in non-Arabic is permissible for both capable and incapable. The Ṣāḥibayn: only for the incapable (ʿājiz).
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilāf, not between madhāhib.
3
Trace the proofs (dalīl)
For Abū Ḥanīfa: meaning is the essence; obligation is according to capacity. For the Ṣāḥibayn: the wording is part of the Qur’an’s miracle; abandoning it without necessity is not allowed.
Both rely on the same principle of taklīf bi-ḥasab al-wus‘ but differ in how they weigh wording vs. meaning.
4
Examine the retraction claim
Some later scholars (e.g., al-‘Aynī, al-Ṭarābulusī) claim Abū Ḥanīfa retracted to the Ṣāḥibayn’s view. The author of ‘Umdat al-Ri‘āya rejects this as unproven.
The retraction claim affects the fatwa hierarchy but is contested.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf)
If a capable person recites the Qur’an in Persian, the prayer is valid per Abū Ḥanīfa but invalid per the Ṣāḥibayn. The later muftā bihī follows the Ṣāḥibayn due to caution.
This directly impacts the validity of ṣalāh for many Muslims.

[3] His saying: 'or al-Rahman'; and likewise with every attribute that is not used except for Allah, like al-Khaliq and al-Razzaq. And if it is used for other than Allah, or nothing similar exists in the Quran, or it resembles the speech of people, like al-Rahim and al-Hakim and al-Karim, one does not become a participant (in the takbir) with it. And if he said 'in other than Arabic' it would be more appropriate, because the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding the permissibility of the takbir, recitation, and other dhikr in it, even if he is not unable to [recite] Arabic according to him, and its impermissibility according to them except for the one who is unable, is not specific to the Persian tongue, but rather it includes it, and the Indian, Turkish, Roman, and other languages, as the commentators of 'al-Hidayah' verified, and they refuted those who thought the disagreement is only about Persian. As for other than it, it is not permissible by agreement. 1] His saying: 'or he recited' – meaning he recited the Quran in prayer in Persian or other than Arabic, and he is unable to recite Arabic. This is by agreement between Abu Hanifa and his two companions. Its reasoning: that although the Quran is a name for both the wording and the meaning, its meaning is not hidden that it is Quran from one aspect, rather it is the more important of the two. So if he is unable to recite the Quran from every aspect, it becomes necessary for him to recite the Quran from one aspect, because obligation is according to capacity. And the well-known [position] of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is that recitation in other than Arabic is permissible for both the one who is able and the one who is unable, based on the consideration of meaning. And his retraction from it to their statement that it is only permissible for the one who is unable has been established, as mentioned in 'al-Talwih' and others. And al-'Ayni in 'Sharh al-Kanz' and al-Tarabulsusi in 'al-Burhan' have affirmed his retraction in the matter of takbir as well, but that is not authentic. And he should seek verification of these discussions along with what they have...
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage addresses a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: the permissibility of performing the takbir (saying 'Allahu Akbar' in substitute forms) and reciting the Quran in languages other than Arabic, such as Persian, Turkish, Hindi, etc. The text first explains that if one substitutes the takbir with other names or attributes of Allah that are exclusively His (like al-Khaliq, al-Razzaq), it is valid; but if the attribute is also used for humans or resembles common speech (like al-Rahim), it does not constitute the takbir. Then it clarifies that the famous ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding reciting in non-Arabic is not limited to Persian but applies to all languages. According to Abu Hanifa, even a person capable of Arabic may recite in another language, because the meaning is what matters. According to the Sahibayn, this is only allowed for one who is unable (عاجز) to recite in Arabic. The text notes that there is a narration that Abu Hanifa retracted his position and adopted the view of his two companions, but the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya sides with those who consider that retraction unproven, citing scholars like al-'Ayni and al-Tarabulsusi who claimed it, but the author rejects it as baseless (ليس بصحيح). The central point is that the dispute remains within the school, and the fatwa on the matter relies on the principle that obligation is based on capacity.

Key Terms
الفارسية (al-fārisiyya): The Persian language; used here as an example of any non-Arabic language.
عاجز (ʿājiz): One who is unable to recite the Arabic wording of the Quran or dhikr due to inability or a valid excuse.
القراءة بغير العربية (al-qirāʾa bi-ghayr al-ʿarabiyya): Reciting the Quran or other prescribed adhkar in a language other than Arabic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only for travelers

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on non-Arabic recitation; the later mufta bihi permits it only for those unable to recite Arabic, but the original view of Abu Hanifa is broader.

Reciting Quran in Non-Arabic and Substituting Takbir
Intra-Hanafi dispute on non-Arabic recitation and takbir substitution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on reciting Quran in non-Arabic and substituting takbir, distinguish the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
The meaning of Quran is primary but the wording is inseparable; thus non-Arabic recitation is only valid for the incapable.

This lesson unpacks a key intra-Hanafi dispute: can a person recite Quran in a language other than Arabic during prayer? Imam Abu Hanifa originally allowed it for all, while his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) restricted it to those incapable of Arabic. The text also addresses substituting the takbir with other glorifying phrases. We will trace the arguments and determine the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts in dispute
There are two acts: (1) reciting Quran in a non-Arabic language during prayer, and (2) replacing 'Allahu Akbar' with other words of glorification. Note that the dispute also extends to other remembrances like tashahhud and Friday sermon.
The passage clarifies the dispute applies to all languages, not just Persian.
2
State Abu Hanifa's original position and its basis
Abu Hanifa allowed non-Arabic recitation for both capable and incapable, based on the principle that the meaning of Quran is the primary consideration. Obligation is according to capacity.
His proof: al-ibrah li al-ma'na.
3
State Sahibayn's position and its basis
Sahibayn said it is impermissible for the capable but valid for the incapable. They argued that Quran is both wording and meaning; the wording has inimitability (i'jaz) and cannot be replaced.
Sahibayn's view is the mufta bihi.
4
Analyze the retraction report (ruju')
Some sources claim Abu Hanifa retracted to Sahibayn's view, but later Hanafi masters like al-Haskafi and Ibn Abidin deny this, citing that major texts (Hidayah, etc.) only mention the dispute without retraction.
This affects which view is considered the madhhab's position.
5
Apply the ruling to takbir substitution
The text permits substituting 'Allahu Akbar' with any wording indicating pure glorification (e.g., 'Allah is Great'), but not mixed with supplication like 'O Allah forgive me'. This follows the same principle of considering meaning.
Also applies to other acts: slaughter, Friday sermon, tashahhud — permissible (but disliked) for capable according to Abu Hanifa; impermissible according to Sahibayn.

[٢] ( ويضع يمينه على شماله " وما عليها من رسالتي آكام النفائس في أداء الأذكار بلسان (الفارس) (١) [١] قوله : أو ذبح وسمى بها ؛ أي قال : الله عند ذبح الحيوان بالفارسية، بسم وكذا إذا خطب في الجمعة بالفارسية أو تشهد في الصلاة بالفارسية، جاز كل ذلك عنده لكن مع كراهة، صرح في «النهاية» وغيرها، وعندهما لا يجوز للقادر على العربية.
Or he recited due to an excuse being incapable of it, the wording used among its people. If he said: 'in other than Arabic' it would be better. Indeed, the difference between Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding the permissibility of the takbir, recitation, and other remembrances in it, even if he is not incapable of Arabic according to him, and its impermissibility according to them except for the incapable, is not specific to the Persian language, but rather applies to it and the Indian, Turkish, Roman, and other languages as verified by the commentators of al-Hidayah, and they refuted those who thought the difference is only in Persian; as for other than it, it is impermissible by consensus. His saying: 'or recited' means he recited the Quran in prayer in Persian or other than it, other than Arabic, and he is incapable of reciting Arabic; this is by agreement between Abu Hanifa and his two companions. Its reasoning: The Quran, although a name for both the arrangement and the meaning, its meaning is not hidden that it is Quran in one respect; rather it is the more important of the two. So if he is incapable of reciting the Quran from every aspect, it becomes necessary upon him to recite the Quran from one aspect because obligation is according to capacity. The famous view of Abu Hanifa's madhhab is that recitation in other than Arabic is permissible for the capable and the incapable alike, based on the principle that consideration is for the meaning, and it has been established that he retracted from it to their view that it is not permissible except for the incapable, as mentioned in al-Talwih and others. Al-Ayni in Sharh al-Kanz and al-Tarabulusi in al-Burhan affirmed his retraction in the issue of takbir as well, but that is not correct. One should seek verification of these investigations with what they have. Al-Haskafi in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and al-Durr al-Muntaqa pointed out the unsoundness of his retraction to their view, and that al-Ayni and those who followed him erred; Ibn Abidin in Radd al-Muhtar supported this. This is indicated by the apparent wording of the matns, al-Hidayah, al-Binayah, al-Inayah, al-Muhit, etc., which suffice with mentioning the difference in the issue without mentioning the retraction. And Allah knows best. 'Or he slaughtered and mentioned the name of Allah by it, it is permissible, and by 'O Allah forgive me' no. The conclusion is that it is permissible to replace Allahu Akbar with a word that indicates mere glorification, and not mixed with supplication. [2] (And he places his right hand on his left) and what is upon it from my two treatises 'Akam al-Nafa'is' on performing remembrances in the Persian language. [1] His saying: 'or he slaughtered and mentioned the name of Allah by it' means he said 'Bismillah' in Persian when slaughtering an animal. Similarly, if he delivers the Friday sermon in Persian or says the tashahhud in prayer in Persian, all that is permissible according to him but with dislike, as stated in al-Nihayah and others; according to them, it is not permissible for one capable of Arabic.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the matn discussing two related rulings: reciting Quran in non-Arabic and substituting the takbir (Allahu Akbar) with other words in prayer. The author first clarifies that the dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) applies to all non-Arabic languages, not just Persian. Abu Hanifa's original view allowed recitation in non-Arabic for both the capable and the incapable, based on the principle that the meaning of Quran is the primary consideration. However, the text reports that he later retracted to the position of the Sahibayn, who restrict non-Arabic recitation to the incapable. The author then notes that some later scholars (al-Haskafi, Ibn Abidin) deny the retraction, maintaining that Abu Hanifa's original view remains. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the Sahibayn's position, as it is the most precautionary and widely accepted for fatwa. The passage then moves to the issue of substituting the takbir: it is permissible to replace 'Allahu Akbar' with any wording that purely indicates glorification (e.g., 'Allah is Great') and not mixed with supplication like 'O Allah forgive me'. This is based on the same principle of considering meaning. The commentary on 'or he slaughtered' extends the ruling to other acts like Friday sermon and tashahhud: according to Abu Hanifa, they are permissible but disliked in non-Arabic for the capable; according to the Sahibayn, they are impermissible for the capable.

Key Terms
القراءة بغير العربية (al-qira'ah bi-ghayr al-'arabiyyah): Reciting Quran in a language other than Arabic in prayer
الرجوع (al-ruju'): The alleged retraction of Abu Hanifa from his original view to that of the Sahibayn
التكبير (al-takbir): Saying 'Allahu Akbar' in prayer or other contexts

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Anyone capable of Arabic
B) Only those incapable of Arabic
C) Both capable and incapable
D) No one at all

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that reciting Quran in a non-Arabic language invalidates the prayer for a person capable of Arabic, due to the inseparable nature of wording and meaning in the Quran.

Placing the Right Hand on the Left in Prayer
Ruling on sunnah hand placement with hadith evidence and intra-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on placing the right hand in prayer and distinguish between the methods of wad' and qabd based on hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Placing right hand on left in prayer is sunnah muakkadah, with flexibility between placing and grasping.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses a seemingly simple act: where to place the hands during prayer. While it is a sunnah practice, the exact method has been a subject of scholarly discussion within the Hanafi school. The text presents the hadith evidence and the preferred view (al-mukhtar) after considering different opinions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is placing the right hand on the left during prayer. The ruling is sunnah muakkadah (highly recommended), not obligatory. Omission does not invalidate the prayer.
The text begins with the phrase 'wa yada'u yaminahu 'ala shimalihi' (and he places his right hand on his left).
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr, among others, where the Prophet ﷺ placed his right hand on his left. These narrations are reported by Abu Dawud, Ibn Khuzaymah, Ibn Hibban, and al-Nasa'i.
The hadith are ahad (single-reporter), but their multiple chains strengthen the evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit action (fi'l) of the Prophet is proof of sunnah. His constant practice indicates a strong recommendation. There is no explicit command, but the consistent act implies sunnah muakkadah.
Al-Marghinani's pronoun system: here 'he said' refers to al-Quduri's matn.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars advocated combining wad' (placing) and qabd (grasping) simultaneously by placing the palm and encircling the wrist. Others, like al-Shurunbulali and al-Nabulsi, rejected this as a forced interpretation. The preferred view (al-mukhtar) is to alternate: sometimes place, sometimes grasp.
The hadith of Wa'il supports placing on the forearm, not just the wrist.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Later authorities (e.g., al-Ayni in al-Binayah) consider the hadith of Wa'il as the most authentic. The mufta bihi is to place the right palm on the back of the left hand on the left forearm, without necessarily grasping the wrist.
The tarjih authority is al-Ayni, and the authority weight is zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : ويضع يمينه على شماله ؛ ورد في بعض الروايات : «أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وضع يمينه على شماله» أخرجه أبو داود وابن خزيمة وابن حبان، وفي بعضها: «إنّه قبض بيمينه على شماله» أخرجه النسائي ، وفي بعضها: «أخذ شماله بيمينه»، أخرجه أبو داود وابن حبان. فذهب بعض مشايخنا إلى اختيار الجمع بأن يضع باطن كفه اليمنى على ظاهرِ كفه اليُسرى، ويُحلّق بالخنصر والإبهام على الرسغ ، ليتحقق القبضُ والوضع كلاهما. وأورد عليه الشرنبلالي والنابلسي وغيرهما بأن هذا جمع خارج عن المذهب والأحاديث، والمختار أن يضع تارةً ويقبض تارة، ثم محل الوضع قيل ذراعه الأيسر، وقيل مفصله الأيسر، قال في «البناية»: وهو الأصح ، ويوافقه حديث وائل : «أنه وضع يده اليمنى على ظهر كفّه اليسرى والرسغ والساعد».
[2] His saying: 'And he places his right hand on his left hand.' It is narrated in some traditions that the Prophet ﷺ placed his right hand on his left hand, reported by Abu Dawud, Ibn Khuzaymah, and Ibn Hibban. In some narrations: 'He grasped with his right hand on his left hand,' reported by al-Nasa'i. And in some: 'He took his left hand with his right hand,' reported by Abu Dawud and Ibn Hibban. Some of our scholars chose to combine by placing the palm of his right hand on the back of his left hand, and he encircles with the little finger and thumb on the wrist, so that both grasping and placing are realized. Al-Shurunbulali, al-Nabulsi, and others objected that this combination is outside the madhhab and the hadiths. The chosen view is to place sometimes and grasp sometimes. Then the location of placement: it is said on his left forearm, and it is said on his left joint. In al-Binayah, it is said: that is the most correct. And it is supported by the hadith of Wa'il: 'That he placed his right hand on the back of his left hand, the wrist, and the forearm.'
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage explains the sunnah act of placing the right hand on the left in prayer. The commentator cites multiple hadith narrations that describe the Prophet's practice: either placing (wad') or grasping (qabd). Hanafi scholars differed on how to interpret these narrations. Some advocated a combination: placing the palm on the back of the left hand while encircling the wrist with the little finger and thumb to achieve both wad' and qabd. However, later authorities like al-Shurunbulali and al-Nabulsi rejected this as a forced interpretation outside the authentic hadiths. The preferred view (mukhtar) is to alternate between placing and grasping on different occasions. As for where to place the hand, the correct position according to al-Binayah is on the left forearm (dhira') or the left joint (mafsil). This is supported by the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr, who saw the Prophet place his right hand on the back of his left hand, wrist, and forearm. In practice, the Hanafi madhhab adopts the position that the right hand should be placed on the left, with the palm on the back of the hand and the fingers extended, without necessarily encircling the wrist.

Key Terms
الوضع (al-wad'): Placing the right hand on the left without grasping
القبض (al-qabd): Grasping the left wrist with the right hand
الرسغ (al-rasagh): The wrist joint

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the preferred Hanafi view on the placement of the right hand in prayer?
A) Always grasp the left wrist with the right hand
B) Place the right palm on the back of the left hand on the forearm
C) Alternate between placing and grasping on different occasions
D) Keep the hands at the sides

Key Takeaway
The sunnah of hand placement in prayer is flexible: the Hanafi mufta bihi is to place on the left forearm, with the option to alternate between placing and grasping.

Using Persian in Worship: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi ruling on substituting Arabic dhikr with Persian equivalents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on substituting Arabic dhikr with Persian, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of their disagreement.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits Persian in worship with dislike; the Sahibayn require Arabic for those able.

This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi difference about using Persian phrases in place of Arabic ritual formulas. Abu Hanifa (رحمه الله) permits it but with karahah (dislike), while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) forbid it for anyone capable of Arabic. The practical outcome is significant: according to Abu Hanifa, the act is valid though disliked; according to the Sahibayn, it is invalid and must be repeated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts discussed
The passage mentions four acts: saying the takbir (Allahu Akbar), slaughtering an animal (saying Bismillah), delivering the Friday sermon (khutbah), and reciting the tashahhud in prayer. In each, Persian may be substituted for the Arabic formula.
The acts are worship-related and involve specific Arabic phrases.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Abu Hanifa holds that substituting Persian is permissible (ja'iz) but with karahah (dislike). The phrase 'جاز كل ذلك عنده لكن مع كراهة' indicates validity but disapproval.
The karahah here is tanzihiyyah (slight dislike), not prohibition.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that it is not permissible (la yajuz) for a person who is capable of Arabic (القادر على العربية). Their condition is key: if one knows Arabic, Persian is invalid.
The Sahibayn's position is stricter, requiring Arabic for validity.
4
Identify the condition that differentiates
The difference hinges on the ability to speak Arabic. Abu Hanifa does not condition validity on Arabic ability; the meaning suffices. The Sahibayn require exact Arabic wording if one can speak it.
This reflects different views on the role of language in ritual acts.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a person who knows Arabic uses Persian in these acts. According to Abu Hanifa, the act is valid (sahih) but disliked (makruh). According to the Sahibayn, the act is invalid (batil) and must be repeated.
This difference affects whether the prayer or slaughter is accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو ذبح وسمى بها جاز، وباللهم اغفر لي لا ، فالحاصل أنه يجوز أن يُبَدِّلَ اللَّهُ أكبر بذكر ما يدلُّ على مجرَّدِ التَّعظيم، ولا يشوب بالدعاء. [١] قوله : أو ذبح وسمى بها ؛ أي قال : الله عند ذبح الحيوان بالفارسية، بسم وكذا إذا خطب في الجمعة بالفارسية أو تشهد في الصلاة بالفارسية، جاز كل ذلك عنده لكن مع كراهة، صرح في «النهاية» وغيرها، وعندهما لا يجوز للقادر على العربية.
Or he slaughtered and mentioned (the name of Allah) with it, it is permissible, and with 'Allahumma ighfir li' it is not. So the conclusion is that it is permissible to substitute 'Allahu Akbar' with a mention that indicates mere glorification, and not mixed with supplication. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is not permissible for one who is capable of Arabic.

Commentary
This passage addresses the intra-Hanafi difference regarding the permissibility of using Persian phrases in place of Arabic dhikr in worship. The matn likely states that if one slaughters and says 'Bismillah' in Persian, it is permissible, but saying 'Allahumma ighfir li' (a supplication) is not permissible as a substitute for takbir. The commentary explains that Abu Hanifa (رحمه الله) allows using Persian for the takbir, slaughtering, Friday sermon, and tashahhud, though with karahah (dislike). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that it is not permissible for someone who can speak Arabic. This difference stems from differing views on the obligation of using Arabic in ritual acts. Abu Hanifa considers the meaning sufficient, while the Sahibayn require the exact Arabic wording to preserve the ritual form.

Key Terms
الكراهة (al-karāhah): Dislike, a legal category between permissible and prohibited. Here it indicates that the act is valid but discouraged.
عنده (ʿindahu): Literally 'with him' meaning the opinion of Abu Hanifa.
عندهما (ʿindahumā): According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa permits Persian in worship with dislike; the Sahibayn require Arabic for those who can speak it, making the act invalid otherwise.
